-٢- كلمةً
طَلبةِ السَّنَةِ الثَّالثةِ بكُلِّيَّةِ الشَّريعة الإسلاميَّةِ بِالجامعة الأزهريَّةِ
مِنَ العلوم ذاتِ الأهميَّةِ في كُلِّيَّةِ الشَّريعةِ عِلْمُ المنطقِ، وَمِنَ الكتب الَّتى أُلّفَتْ في هذا العِلْم ؛ شرحُ الخَبيصي على مَثْنِ التَّهذيب، وعلى هذا الشرح الجليل كتب الشَّيخُ العطَّارُ والشَّيخُ الدُّسوقي حاشِيَتَيْهِمَا ، إلا أنَّ فيها بعض تعقيداتٍ في الأسلوب، واعتراضاتٍ كثيرةً تُوجَّهُ على الشَّرحِ وترد على المتنِ لأَوْهَى الأسباب، مِمَّا كاد يخرج الكتاب عن الغرض الَّذي أُلْفَ مِن أجلِهِ، فَكُنَّا نَحنُ الطُلاب نلقى عناءً كبيراً، ومشقَّةٌ عظيمةً في استخراج ما في سطور الحاشِيَتَيْنِ مِنَ الفوائدِ، وَيَعلمُ الله وحدَهُ كَمْ كُنَّا نقرأُ المسألة، فإذا ما فرغنا منها؛ لا نجد لها أثراً في عقولنا، فَنستعيدُ قراءَتَها مَرَّاتٍ وقد نضطر في النّهايةِ إلى استظهار هذه الكلمات التي هي بالرموز في نظرنا أشبهُ، وَبِالطَّلاسم عندنا أقرب فرأى أستاذنا الجليل العلامة الشَّيخُ مُحمَّد عبد المجيدِ الشَّرنوبي؛ المدرِّسُ بالكُلِّيَّةِ ما يصيبُ الطَّلبة من عناء وَمَشقَّةٍ في تحصيل هذا العِلْم، فتقدَّمَ مُتطوّعاً الشركة مكتبة ومطبعة حضراتِ مُصطفى البابي الحلبي وأولاده والتي تعهدت بإخراج هذا الشرح بحاشيتي الدُّسوقي والعطّار، وأخذ على نفسِهِ تصحيحَهُ والتّعليق عليه، ولم يقصد أستاذنا عملِهِ منفعة ماديَّةٌ، بل ضَحَى بِثَمينِ وَقتِهِ، وساعاتِ راحتِهِ، وعلق على الشرح والحاشيتين معاً، بما يدلُّ على غزارة مادة، وسعة اطلاع، وعظيم رغبة في خدمة العِلْم والتعليم، فتراه قد وقف في كتابهِ مَوقِفَ المنصفِ؛ فَلَا هوَ يَعْمَطُ مِنَ العطَّارِ وَلَا اَلدُّسوقي، وَلَا هو ينقصُ مِن شأنِ الخَبيصي، وَلَا هو يزهو بِعِلْمِهِ ويفتخرُ بتعليقِهِ، بِلِ التزم حدَّ الوسط في كتابتِهِ معَ تواضع وأدب وعِلْمٍ فيَّاض، فجاءَ تَعليقه على الوجهِ الَّذي كُنَّا نتمنَّاهُ وافياً بالغرض الَّذي كُنَّا في حاجةٍ إليهِ، مُبيِّنَاً للموضوعات التي كُنَّا نَيْنُ مِنْ هَوْلِ صعوبَتِهَا .
من
Glorious commendations
-2- Word
From the third-year students at the Faculty of Islamic Law at Al-Azhar University
Among the important sciences in the Faculty of Law is logic, and among the books written on this science is the commentary of al-Khabbisi on the matn of Tahzeeb. On this esteemed commentary, Shaykh al-'Attar and Shaykh al-Dasuqi wrote their two glosses, but they contain some complexities in style and many objections directed at the commentary and responded to the matn for the flimsiest reasons, nearly taking the book away from the purpose for which it was written. So we students encountered great trouble and immense hardship in extracting the benefits from the lines of the two glosses. Allah alone knows how many times we would read an issue, and when we finished it, we would find no trace of it in our minds. So we would repeat its reading many times, and sometimes we were forced in the end to memorize these words, which in our view were more like symbols and closer to talismans. Then our great teacher, the eminent scholar Shaykh Muhammad 'Abd al-Majid al-Sharnubi, the lecturer at the Faculty, saw what the students were suffering in terms of trouble and hardship in mastering this science. So he volunteered to the company, the library and printing press of the esteemed Mustafa al-Babi al-Halabi and Sons, which undertook to produce this commentary with the glosses of al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar, and he took upon himself its correction and annotation. Our teacher did not intend material benefit from his work, but rather sacrificed his precious time and hours of rest, and annotated the commentary and the two glosses together, indicating abundant knowledge, wide learning, and great desire to serve knowledge and education. So you see him in his book taking the position of a fair judge: he neither distorts from al-'Attar nor al-Dasuqi, nor does he diminish the status of al-Khabbisi, nor does he boast of his knowledge or take pride in his annotation. Rather, he adhered to the middle course in his writing, with humility, politeness, and abundant knowledge. So his annotation came in the manner we hoped for, fulfilling the purpose we needed, clarifying the subjects over which we groaned due to the terror of their difficulty.
From
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a commendation from third-year students at Al-Azhar University's Faculty of Law. They begin by stating the importance of logic and mention that al-Khabbisi's commentary on Tahzeeb is a key text. However, they note that the glosses of al-'Attar and al-Dasuqi, though esteemed, are complex and contain many weak objections that obscure the purpose of the book. The students describe their struggle to understand and retain the material, often having to memorize without comprehension. Their teacher, al-Sharnubi, recognized their hardship and voluntarily undertook to correct and annotate the text, working without material gain. The students praise his balanced approach, fairness, and humility, stating that his annotations clarify the difficult topics. This passage serves as a student testimonial for the edition.

Key Terms
الشرح
الحاشية
التعقيدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the students, what was the primary problem with the existing glosses of al-'Attar and al-Dasuqi?
A) They were too brief and lacked detail.
B) They contained many weak objections and complex style.
C) They were written in a foreign language.
D) They contradicted the original matn entirely.

Key Takeaway
The students' commendation underscores that a commentary should illuminate, not obscure, the original text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Based on the students' description, list three criteria that a good commentary on a logical text should meet. Then, find an example of a commentary (e.g., al-Sabban's hashiya on al-Khabbisi) and evaluate it against your criteria.
?

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Role of Commentaries in Islamic Logic Education
Students praise their teacher's commentary on al-Tahdhib and describe their difficulties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main components of the commentary tradition (matn, sharh, hashiyah) and explain why clarity in commentary is crucial for learning logic.

Core Idea
A confusing commentary can be as harmful as no commentary at all.

This passage is a student commendation praising a teacher’s work to clarify a notoriously difficult set of glosses on a standard logic text. It reveals the real-world struggle behind learning logic from traditional texts and the vital role of clear exposition. In this lesson, we will dissect the structure of the commentary tradition and understand why al-Sharnubi’s annotated edition was so valued.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text (matn)
Locate the original text that is being commented on. Here it is al-Tahdhib, a standard work on logic.
2
Identify the first-level commentary (sharh)
Find the commentary that explains the matn. Here it is Sharh al-Khabisi on al-Tahdhib.
3
Identify the second-level commentaries (hashiyah)
These are glosses on the sharh. Here they are Hashiyat al-Attar and Hashiyat al-Dasuqi.
The chain is matn → sharh → hashiyah.
4
Recognize the problem with the glosses
The students complain that the glosses contain complexities and weak objections, making them nearly impossible to understand. They had to memorize without comprehension.
5
Recognize the solution offered by al-Sharnubi
Al-Sharnubi produced an annotated edition that corrected and clarified the commentary and glosses, striking a fair balance and making the content accessible.
His approach was neither to disparage previous scholars nor to show off his own knowledge.

طَلبةِ السَّنَةِ الثَّالثةِ بكُلِّيَّةِ الشَّريعة الإسلاميَّةِ بِالجامعة الأزهريَّةِ
مِنَ العلوم ذاتِ الأهميَّةِ في كُلِّيَّةِ الشَّريعةِ عِلْمُ المنطقِ، وَمِنَ الكتب الَّتى أُلّفَتْ في هذا العِلْم ؛ شرحُ الخَبيصي على مَتْنِ التَّهذيب، وعلى هذا الشرح الجليل كتب الشَّيخُ العطَّارُ والشَّيخُ الدُّسوقي حاشِيَتَيْهِمَا ، إلا أنَّ فيها بعض تعقيداتٍ في الأسلوب، واعتراضاتٍ كثيرةً تُوجَّهُ على الشَّرحِ وترد على المتنِ لأَوْهَى الأسباب، مِمَّا كاد يخرج الكتاب عن الغرض الَّذي أُلْفَ مِن أجلِهِ، فَكُنَّا نَحنُ الطُلاب نلقى عناءً كبيراً، ومشقَّةٌ عظيمةً في استخراج ما في سطور الحاشِيَتَيْنِ مِنَ الفوائدِ، وَيَعلمُ الله وحدَهُ كَمْ كُنَّا نقرأُ المسألة، فإذا ما فرغنا منها؛ لا نجد لها أثراً في عقولنا، فَنستعيدُ قراءَتَها مَرَّاتٍ وقد نضطر في النّهايةِ إلى استظهار هذه الكلمات التي هي بالرموز في نظرنا أشبهُ، وَبِالطَّلاسم عندنا أقرب فرأى أستاذنا الجليل العلامة الشَّيخُ مُحمَّد عبد المجيدِ الشَّرنوبي ؛ المدرِّسُ بالكُلِّيَّةِ ما يصيبُ الطَّلبة من عناء وَمَشقَّةٍ في تحصيل هذا العِلْم، فتقدَّمَ مُتطوّعاً إلى شركة مكتبة ومطبعة حضراتِ مصطفى البابي الحلبي وأولاده والتي تعهدت بإخراج هذا الشرح بحاشيتي الدُّسوقي والعطّار، وأخذ على نفسِهِ تصحيحَهُ والتّعليق عليه، ولم يقصد أستاذنا عملِهِ منفعة ماديَّةٌ، بل ضَحَى بِثَمينِ وَقتِهِ، وساعاتِ راحتِهِ، وعلق على الشرح والحاشيتين معاً، بما يدلُّ على غزارة مادة، وسعة اطلاع، وعظيم رغبة في خدمة العِلْم والتعليم، فتراه قد وقف في كتابهِ مَوقِفَ المنصفِ ؛ فَلَا هوَ يَعْمَطُ مِنَ العطَّارِ وَلَا اَلدُّسوقي، وَلَا هو ينقصُ مِن شأنِ الخَبيصي ، وَلَا هو يزهو بِعِلْمِهِ ويفتخرُ بتعليقِهِ ، بَلِ التزم حدَّ الوسط في كتابتِهِ معَ تواضع وأدب وعِلْمٍ فيَّاض، فجاءَ تَعليقه على الوجهِ الَّذي كُنَّا نتمنَّاهُ وافياً بالغرض الَّذي كُنَّا في حاجةٍ إليهِ، مُبيِّنَاً للموضوعات التي كُنَّا نَيْنُ مِنْ هَوْلِ صعوبَتِهَا . من
لِذَا؛ نتقدَّمُ لأستاذنا بهذه الكلمة، ونعلنُ أنَّها دون ما يستحقُ مِنَ التَّقدير والإعجاب، وأقل ما يجب نحوَهُ مِنَ المدح والثناءِ، فَأَستاذنَا الشَّرنوبي؛ إِنْ لم يكن لهُ غيرُ فَضْلِ فَتْحِ بابِ التَّنافس بين الأساتذة؛ لَكَفَاهُ مَدْحاً، وكأَنَّنا نرى الآنَ بِعَينِ الغَيْبِ عشراتٍ مِنَ الكُتُبِ الثَّمينةِ قَدْ طُبِعَتْ طَبْعاً مُتقناً، وشرحها أساتِذَتُنَا شَرِحاً يتناسب مع روح العصرِ ، بَلْ كَأَنَّنَا بِهَا وَقَد فتَحْنَاها فَوَجَدْنَاهَا تُنيرُ الطَّرِيقَ إلى الصَّوابِ، وتهدي النَّاسَ إلى الخير، وتُرشدُهُمْ إلى مواضع العظمة في دينهم وعلومهم، وتعلنُ في الوقتِ نفسِهِ عَمَّا في الأزهرِ مِنْ عقول ناضجة وعلوم واسعة، وأفكار سليمة . وأخيراً؛ نتقدَّمُ لِأُستاذنَا بالشُّكرِ، ونُهنّتُهُ بِعِلْمِهِ هَذَا الَّذِي أَرضَى بِهِ اللَّهَ وَالعِلْمَ وإخوانَة الأساتذة والطَّلبة، فإلى الأمام يا أستاذنَا ، أَنْسُج على منوال أبيكَ الصَّالِحِ، الذي ألَّفَ الكتب العظيمةَ ، وَعَمَّ الانتفاع بها في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها، وأخرج لَنَا مِنْ ثمار شجرتِكُم الطَّيّبة؛ شجرةِ العِلْم والدِّينِ مَا يعزي النفوس، ويُشبعُ الأرواحَ، وَأَرِنَا لَآلِئَ بحرِكُم المملوء بالعِلْم والحكمة، حتَّى نهتدي بِكُمْ وَنسترشد بِعِلْمِكُم وأدبكُم، والسَّلامُ عليكم ورحمةُ اللهِ . عنهم : عباس متولّي حمادة، عبد السَّلام الكاشف، محمد مصطفى جاد، محمد الحسيني سويدان، عبد السَّلامِ عجلان.
Splendid commendations. A word from the students of the third year at the College of Islamic Law at al-Azhar University. Among the important subjects in the College of Law is the science of logic, and among the books authored on this subject is al-Khabisi's commentary on the text of al-Tahdhib. On this sublime commentary, Sheikh al-Attar and Sheikh al-Dasuqi wrote their glosses, except that they contain some complexities in style and many objections directed against the commentary and responding to the text for the weakest reasons, to the point that the book almost departed from the purpose for which it was authored. So we, the students, encountered great trouble and immense difficulty in extracting the benefits from the lines of the two glosses. God alone knows how many times we would read a problem, and when we finished it, we would find no trace of it in our minds. So we would reread it repeatedly, and at the end we might be forced to memorize these words which to us seemed more like symbols and closer to talismans. Then our noble teacher, the eminent scholar Sheikh Muhammad Abd al-Majid al-Sharnubi, the lecturer at the College, saw what the students were suffering of trouble and difficulty in acquiring this science. So he volunteered to approach the library and press company of the gentlemen Mustafa al-Babi al-Halabi and his sons, which undertook to publish this commentary with the two glosses of al-Dasuqi and al-Attar. He took upon himself its correction and annotation, and our teacher did not intend any material benefit from his work; rather, he sacrificed precious time and hours of rest, and he annotated the commentary and the two glosses together in a way that indicates abundance of material, breadth of knowledge, and great desire to serve knowledge and education. So you see him in his book taking the position of a fair-minded person: he neither oppresses al-Attar nor al-Dasuqi, nor does he detract from al-Khabisi's standing, nor does he show off his knowledge or boast of his annotation. Rather, he adhered to the middle path in his writing, with humility, decorum, and abundant knowledge. So his annotation came in the manner we had wished, fulfilling the purpose we needed, clarifying the topics that we [suffered] from the terror of their difficulty. Therefore, we present this word to our teacher and declare that it is less than what he deserves of appreciation and admiration, and the least that is due to him of praise and commendation. Our teacher al-Sharnubi, if he had no other merit than opening the door of competition among teachers, that would suffice as praise. As if we now see with the unseen eye dozens of precious books printed in a precise manner, and our teachers explain them in a way befitting the spirit of the age. Rather, as if we have opened them and found them illuminating the path to truth, guiding people to goodness, directing them to the positions of greatness in their religion and sciences, and at the same time declaring what al-Azhar possesses of mature minds, vast sciences, and sound ideas. Finally, we present our thanks to our teacher and congratulate him on this knowledge of his, by which he pleased God, knowledge, and his fellow teachers and students. So forward, our teacher, follow the path of your righteous father, who authored great books and whose benefit spread throughout the East and West of the earth. Bring out for us from the fruits of your good tree – the tree of knowledge and religion – that which comforts souls and satisfies spirits, and show us the pearls of your sea filled with knowledge and wisdom, that we may be guided by you and seek guidance from your knowledge and decorum. Peace be upon you and God's mercy. From them: Abbas Mutawalli Hamada, Abd al-Salam al-Kashif, Muhammad Mustafa Jad, Muhammad al-Husayni Suwaydan, Abd al-Salam Ajlan.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a preface (taqriz) written by a group of students at the College of Islamic Law at al-Azhar University, praising their teacher Sheikh Muhammad Abd al-Majid al-Sharnubi for his work on a commentary on al-Tahdhib, a standard logic text. They open by stating that logic is an important subject in their curriculum. They mention that the commentary by al-Khabisi on al-Tahdhib, and the glosses by al-Attar and al-Dasuqi on that commentary, are the main sources. However, they complain that these glosses contain complexities and unnecessary objections that obscure the purpose of the book. The students describe their struggle: they would read a problem repeatedly but could retain nothing, forcing them to memorize the text without understanding, as if it were mere symbols or talismans. Seeing their difficulty, their teacher al-Sharnubi volunteered to produce a corrected and annotated edition of the commentary with the two glosses, working without material gain. He took a balanced approach, neither disparaging the previous scholars nor boasting of his own learning. The students express that his work met their needs, clarifying the topics they found extremely difficult. They then thank him profusely, praising his sacrifice of time and his fair-mindedness. They express hope that this work opens a door for competition among teachers to produce more such books. They conclude by greeting him and listing their names.

المنطق
التَّهذيب
الحاشية
الشرح
المتن

Key Takeaway
The commentary tradition in Islamic logic requires clarity at each level; obscure glosses can hinder understanding rather than help.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Find a contemporary example from any Islamic science (e.g., fiqh, tafsir) where a confusing commentary or gloss exists. Explain how a revised edition could help students, using the logic of this passage.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
Reading a Taqriz: Praise for Logic in Islamic Scholarship
A sheikh lauds the author's critical edition of glosses on a logic commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key rhetorical features of a taqriz and explain how it asserts the authority of logic in Islamic education.

Core Idea
A taqriz frames a work's significance and reveals assumptions about the value of logic in Islam.

This passage is a formal commendation (taqriz) from Sheikh Hasan Talab al-Bakri for an edition of glosses on a logic commentary. Though it may seem like mere praise, it actually reveals important assumptions about the value of logic in the Islamic sciences. Let's unpack how the sheikh positions logic as the 'criterion of sciences' and the author as a model scholar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the speaker and context
Who is the sheikh, and what group does he represent? Why is he writing this taqriz? The passage mentions he speaks 'on behalf of the first-year students of the Faculty of Islamic Sharia' at Al-Azhar.
This indicates the institutional context and the intended audience.
2
Locate the specific work praised
What exactly did the author do? The sheikh says he 'devoted himself to refining two noble glosses on the commentary of al-Khubaysi'—a well-known logical commentary.
The work is a critical edition, not an original treatise.
3
Extract attributes assigned to logic
What phrases describe logic? The sheikh calls it 'the criterion of sciences' and 'the measure of thought', indicating its epistemological primacy in the Islamic sciences.
This shows how logic was valued in the curriculum.
4
Analyze the praise of the author
What qualities of the author are emphasized? The sheikh lauds his 'freedom of opinion', 'independence of thought', 'integrity in truth', and 'careful investigation'. These reflect the ideal scholar in the Islamic tradition.
The praise connects personal virtues to scholarly work.
5
Relate to the broader tradition
How does this taqriz legitimize the study of logic? The sheikh describes the work as a 'blessed revival' and a 'first brick' in building a lofty edifice, linking logic to the preservation of the faith.
This rhetorical move strengthens the authority of logic in religious sciences.

تقاريظ جليلة -٣- كلمةُ الشَّيخ حسن طلب البكري عَنْ طَلَبَةِ السَّنَةِ الْأُوْلَى بِكُلِّيَّةِ الشَّريعة الإسلامية بالجامعة الأزهريَّةِ نهضةٌ مُباركةٌ، ونفحة عطرةٌ، وَروحٌ قويَّةٌ، انبعثَتُ مِنْ أستاذ عظيمٍ بِكُلِّيَّةِ الشَّرِيعَةِ الإسلاميَّةِ ، فَلَقَدْ ضربَ لَنَا مثلاً أعلى في حُرِّيَّةِ الرَّأي، واستقلال الفكر، والنزاهة في الحقِّ، إذ تصدَّى لمعيار العلوم وميزان الفكر، وسبيل الاستدلال، أَلَا وَهُوَ عِلْمُ المنطِقِ، فعني بتنقيح حَاشيتَيْنِ جَليلتَيْنِ على شرح الخبيصي بعد أن سبرَ غورَهُما، وبحثَهُما بحثاً دقيقاً ، وَوازنَ بينَهُما معَ انتصار للحقِّ أينما كانَ، فأزالَ حِجَاباً كثيفاً عن غوامض الكَلِمِ الَّتي كانَتْ تَقِفُ أمامَها قوى الطُّلابِ، وأبانَ رأيَهُ بتعليق طريف يجمعُ العدل والصراحة، في أسلوب ممتع خالٍ عَنِ المواربة والغموض، وإنَّا لَنُحبّي فيهِ هذهِ الرُّوحَ الوثابةَ الَّتي لَا مقصدَ لَهَا سِوَى خدمةِ العِلْمِ والدِّين، والتي تبذلُ كُلَّ مُرتخص وغَالٍ في هذا السبيل. وَلَا عجب، فقد نشأ الشَّيخُ في دوحةِ العِلْم فَتَفَيَّأَ ظِلَّهَا ، وتَرَبَّى في شجرةِ فَارتشفَ مَنَاهِلَهَا ، وأُحيط بسياج الدِّينِ حتَّى امتزجَ حُبُّهُ بِلَحمِهِ وَدَمِهِ، فَجَنِّى هذهِ الثَّمار اليانعة، وأَخَذَ يَنسج على منوال أبيه في الإفادة والاستفادة والتأليف، وتلكَ أُسوة حسنة : «... وَمَنْ يُشَابِه أبهُ فَمَا ظَلَم فَلَقَدْ قصر والدُهُ - رَحِمَهُ الله - حياته على خدمةِ العِلْمِ والدِّينِ والأدب، وقتل وقتهُ في الدَّرْسِ والتَّحْصِيل، فانتفع كثيرٌ مِنَ النَّاسِ بِمَؤلَّفَاتِهِ القَيِّمَةِ النَّادِرَةِ . وتِلْكَ أوَّلُ لَبِنَةٍ يضعها أستاذنا العظيم لبناء ذلك المجدِ الباذخ في تشييد صرح العلم.
Noble Praises: -3- The word of Sheikh Hasan Talab al-Bakri on behalf of the first-year students of the Faculty of Islamic Sharia at Al-Azhar University. A blessed revival, a fragrant breeze, and a strong spirit, emanating from a great teacher at the Faculty of Islamic Sharia. He has set for us a high example in freedom of opinion, independence of thought, and integrity in truth, as he confronted the criterion of sciences and the measure of thought, and the path of inference—indeed it is the science of logic. He devoted himself to refining two noble glosses on the commentary of al-Khubaysi after sounding their depths, examining them with precise investigation, and balancing between them while siding with the truth wherever it was. He removed a thick veil from the obscurities of words that the abilities of students used to stumble over. He clarified his opinion with a unique commentary that combines justice and frankness, in an enjoyable style free from ambiguity. And indeed we admire in him this soaring spirit that has no aim other than serving knowledge and religion, and which spends every available and valuable thing in this path. No wonder, for the sheikh grew up in the garden of knowledge and enjoyed its shade, was nurtured in its tree and drank from its springs, and was surrounded by the fence of religion until its love mixed with his flesh and blood. So he reaped these ripe fruits, and began to weave on the pattern of his father in benefiting others, learning, and authorship. That is a good example: 'Whoever resembles his father does no wrong.' For his father—may God have mercy on him—dedicated his life to serving knowledge, religion, and literature, and killed his time in study and learning, so many people benefited from his valuable rare works. And this is the first brick that our great teacher places in building that lofty glory in erecting the edifice of knowledge.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a formal praise (taqriz) by Sheikh Hasan Talab al-Bakri, speaking on behalf of first-year students of the Faculty of Islamic Sharia. He lauds the author (referred to as 'our teacher') for his work in logic—specifically, for his critical edition and refinement of two glosses on the commentary of al-Khubaysi. The passage emphasizes the importance of logic as the criterion of sciences and the measure of thought. The author's work is presented as a model of freedom, independence, and integrity. The comment also highlights the author's background, being raised in a scholarly and religious environment, and following the footsteps of his father. This sets the stage for the book as a significant contribution to Islamic education.

Understanding Commendatory Poems in Logic Works
Commendatory poem praising the author.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose and typical features of commendatory poems (taqariz) found in the front matter of Islamic scholarly works, particularly logic texts, and distinguish them from logical content.

Core Idea
Commendatory poems honour the author and signal scholarly approval, not logical instruction.

This lesson examines a eulogy poem (qasida 'asma') by Sheikh Fahim Salim al-Maliji, praising the author of a logic text. While such poems are not part of the technical logical content, they are important for understanding the scholarly context and the social validation of the work. By learning to read these poems, you gain insight into how Islamic scholars celebrated and transmitted knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the poem's occasion
Read the heading: it says 'Splendid commendations. 4 - A White Poem.' This is part of a series of commendations at the beginning of the book. Note the author of the poem and his title (teacher at the Cairo Institute).
These details establish the social setting.
2
Analyze the poetic imagery
The poet uses imagery of quenching thirst, illuminating light, clothing in radiant garment, full moon, scale of sciences, mirror of intellects. Each image conveys the book's value: it brings clarity, removes doubt, and organizes knowledge.
Poetic license is used; do not take literally.
3
Determine the poem's function
The poem does not teach any logical concept. Its purpose is to praise the author and his work, to recommend the book to readers, and to express scholarly gratitude. Such commendations (taqariz) are common in Islamic scholarly front matter.
They serve a rhetorical and social role.
4
Distinguish from logical content
Compare the poem's language to the technical terms in the rest of the book (e.g., 'mizan al-'ulum', 'mir'at al-'uqul' are used poetically, not as technical definitions). The poem does not contain arguments or definitions.
Never mistake a eulogy for a logical exposition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقاريظ جليلة
٤ - قَصيدةٌ عَصْمَاء
لِفَضيلةِ الأستاذِ الشَّيخ فهيم سالم المليجي
المدرس بالقسم الثانوي بمعهد القاهرة

لِلَّهِ دَرُكَ مِنْ هُمَامٍ مَاجِدٍ رَوَّيْتَ ظَمْآنَ الْعُلُوْمِ بِمَنْهَلِ
وَأَضَأْتَ نِبْرَاسَ الْحَقَائِقِ لِلنُّهَى فَأَزَلْتَ ظُلْمَةَ رَيْبِهَا كَيْ تَنْجَلِي
أَلْبَسْتَهَا تَوْبَاً قَشِيْبَاً نَاصِعَاً فَغَدَتْ بِهِ تَخْتَالُ بَيْنَ خَمَائِل
وَبَدَا كِتَابُكَ مِثْلَ بَدْرٍ سَاطِعِ فِي أُفُقِ مِيزَانِ الْعُلُومِ المُعْتَلِي
فَأَبَانَ مِنْهَاجَ السَّدَادِ بِحِكْمَةٍ جَلَّتْ كَآيَاتِ الْكِتَابِ المَنْزَلِ
وَبَدَتْ خَفِيَّاتُ الْمَسَائِلِ لِلْأُولى طَلَبُوا الْعُلَى مِثْلَ الصَّبَاحِ الْمُنْجَلِي
شَكَرَتْ عُقُوْلُ الْعَالِمِيْنَ صَنِيْعَكُمْ إِذْ صُغْتَ مِرْآةَ الْعُقُوْلِ بِمِصْقَلِ
Splendid commendations. 4 - A White Poem. By the virtue of the teacher, Sheikh Fahim Salim al-Maliji, teacher at the secondary section of the Cairo Institute. May God bless you, O noble and generous one; you quenched the thirsty of knowledge with a spring. You illuminated the beacon of truths for intellects, so you removed the darkness of its doubt so that it may be cleared. You clothed it with a pure, radiant garment, and it walks proudly among gardens. Your book appeared like a shining full moon in the horizon of the exalted scale of sciences. It clarified the path of rectitude with wisdom that is great like the verses of the revealed book. The hidden aspects of issues became manifest to those who seek high rank, like the morning that dispels darkness. The minds of scholars thanked your work when you fashioned the mirror of intellects with a polishing tool.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a commendatory poem (qasida 'asma') by Sheikh Fahim Salim al-Maliji, praising the author of the logic book (likely Muhammad Abd al-Majid al-Dusuqi or the commentator). The poem uses traditional Arabic poetic imagery to highlight the book's value: it quenches the thirst for knowledge, removes doubts, and clarifies truths. It compares the book to a full moon in the sky of the 'scale of sciences' (a reference to logic as the measure of sciences). The last line thanks the author for polishing the mirror of intellects, i.e., making logic accessible and clear. Such eulogies are common in the front matter of Islamic scholarly works, serving to commend the work and the author's piety and learning.

Key Terms
ميزان العلوم (mīzān al-'ulūm): A metaphor for logic as the scale or standard of the sciences, indicating its role in distinguishing truth from falsehood. [Note: Common in later Islamic logic works; earlier usage may vary.]
مرآة العقول (mir'āt al-'uqūl): A poetic image meaning 'mirror of the intellects', referring to the book as a tool that clarifies rational thought. [Note: Not a standard logical term; purely poetic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To provide a logical syllogism
D) To list the chapters of the book

Key Takeaway
Commendatory poems in Islamic logic texts are cultural endorsements, not logical instruction.

The Role of Panegyric Poems in Islamic Logic Texts
Poem celebrating the teacher and the clarity of the logic text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose of panegyric poems in Islamic logic textbooks and explain how they frame the text's authority and value.

Core Idea
Panegyric poems in logic texts establish teacher authority and frame the text as a spiritual gift.

This lesson examines a panegyric poem found at the beginning of a logic commentary. While it does not teach logical rules, understanding its function helps you appreciate how Islamic scholars introduced their works. The poem praises the teacher and celebrates the text's clarity, setting a respectful and reverent tone. Recognizing such front matter is part of reading scholarly works in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genre
Read the poem and note that it is a panegyric (madih) – a form of praise poetry common in Islamic scholarly front matter. It is not a logical argument but a rhetorical introduction.
2
Find the praised figure
The poem praises 'Muhammad' (the teacher, likely the author of the commentary or a patron) and 'Abd al-Majid'. Identify who is being honored and their role in the logic work.
The praise establishes the teacher's authority and credibility.
3
Analyze the imagery about logic
The poem describes logic as 'difficult' that became 'clear water', 'easy in approach' after hardship. This imagery signals that the text makes logic accessible. Note the metaphors: gardens, bird song, full moon guiding travelers.
The poet uses these images to convey the value and benefit of the work.
4
Explain the purpose
Combine your observations: The poem serves to honor the teacher, attract the reader's goodwill, and frame the logic text as a valuable, clear, and life-giving resource. In the Islamic scholarly tradition, such opening praises are customary to establish the work's importance and the chain of knowledge.
This poem is not mere decoration; it fulfills a social and pedagogical function.

هَذِي الرِّيَاضُ تَجَلَّتْ فِي مَغَانِيْهَا وَغَرَّدَ الطَّيْرُ شَدْوَا فِي نَوَاحِيْهَا وَبُلْبُلُ الْأُنْسِ غَنَّى بَعْدَ هَجْعَتِهِ فَرَجَعَتْ لَعْنَهُ الدُّنْيَا وَمَنْ فِيْهَا وَالْمَنْطِقُ الصَّعْبُ أَضْحَى بَعْدَ شِدَّتِهِ مَاءً زُلَا لَا يُرَوِّي النَّفْسَ يُحْيِيهَا سهلَ المَآخِذِ بَعْدَ الْعُسْرِ قَدْ نُشِرَتْ مِنْهُ دَرَارِيُّ قَدْ كَانَتْ يُغَفِّيْهَا وَالفَضْلُ يَرْجِعُ فِي ذَا كُلِّهِ أَبَداً إِلَى النَّبِيْلِ وَرَبُّ القَوْسِ بَارِيْهَا مُحَمَّدٍ مَنْ سُمِّيَ بِالمُصْطَفَى شَرَفَاً وَمَنْ يُنِيرُ عَلَى الأَيَّامِ دَاجِيْـهَـا غَيْتُ يَجُوْدُ بِتِرْيَاقِ النُّفُوسِ لَهَا فَيَشْمَلُ النَّاسَ قَاصِيْهَا وَدَانِيْـهَـا شُكْرًا أَبَا أَحْمَدٍ عَبْد المجيدِ فَقَدْ أَرْوَيْتَ نَفْسَاً وَكَانَ الْجَهْلُ صَادِيهَا مُحَمَّدٌ أنتَ صِنْوُ البَحْرِ مِنْ قِدَمِ عَبْدُ الـمـجـيْـدِ وَمَنْ سَاوَاهُ تَشْبِيْهَا بدر تألّق لِلسَّارِي وَرُفْقَتِهِ إِنْ ضَلَّ رائِدُهُمْ فَالنُّورُ هَادِيهَا فَانْسُجْ عَلَى سَيْرِهِ حَتَّى نَرَى قَبْساً يَهْدِي سَنَاهُ مَـدَى الأَيَّامِ سَارِيْـهَـا
These gardens have revealed themselves in their meanings, and the bird has sung in its regions. The nightingale of familiarity sang after its slumber, and the curse of this world and those in it returned. The difficult logic, after its severity, has become clear water that quenches not the soul but revives it. Easy in approach after hardship, its stars have been spread out that were previously hiding. The virtue in all this returns forever to the noble one and the Lord of the bow is its Creator. Muhammad, who is named al-Mustafa in honor, and who illuminates the darkest days. A gift that bestows an antidote for souls upon them, and includes people near and far. Thanks, O father of Ahmad, Abd al-Majid, for you have quenched a soul that was thirsting with ignorance. Muhammad, you are like the sea from ancient times, and Abd al-Majid, and whoever compares him to him is likening. A full moon that shines for the night traveler and his company, if their guide strays, the light is their guide. Follow his path until we see a flame whose glow guides the days' traveler.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a panegyric poem (قصيدة) found in the front matter of the commentary. It praises the teacher (likely the author of the commentary or a patron) and celebrates the logic text being made accessible. The poet uses imagery: 'gardens' and 'birds' to symbolize beauty and understanding; 'difficult logic' that has become 'clear water' signifies the text's clarity. The teacher is likened to a noble one, a sea, and a full moon guiding travelers. The poem expresses gratitude for dispelling ignorance and making logic easy.

Understanding the Commentator's Introduction: The Purpose of a Hashiya
Author explains the genesis of the commentary and names his work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of a traditional Islamic commentary preface, including the author's motivation, the names of the source layers, and the title significance.

Core Idea
A commentary's preface reveals the chain of learning and the author's purpose in preserving scholarly additions.

This passage is the opening of al-Dardir's commentary on the Tadhheeb al-Mantiq. It is not a logical argument, but it is crucial for understanding the work's place in the tradition. Here, the author explains how he compiled the notes of his teacher al-Dasuqi and named his work. By studying this preface, you will see how the commentary tradition preserves and transmits knowledge across generations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his source materials
Find the name of the commentator (al-Dardir) and the three layers he mentions: (1) al-Khabisi's original text, (2) al-Janahi's taqrir, and (3) al-Dasuqi's additions.
2
Understand the author's motivation
Why did al-Dardir compile this work? Because al-Dasuqi intended to make an independent hashya but passed away. Al-Dardir extracted and supplemented the notes.
3
Analyze the title
The work is named 'al-Tajrid al-Shafi 'ala Tadhheeb al-Mantiq al-Kafi'. Break down the meaning: 'Tajrid' (abstract), 'Shafi' (sufficient/clear), 'ala' (upon), 'Tadhheeb' (polishing), 'Mantiq' (logic), 'al-Kafi' (the sufficient). Reflect on how the title indicates the work's purpose of 'abstracting' the essence of logic.
4
Connect to the Islamic scholarly tradition
Note how the author prays for benefit and mentions the chain of teachers. This shows the importance of isnad (chain) even in non-hadith texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المحشي
[مقدمة المحشي]
إنَّ أحلَى مَنطق تحلَّى بِهِ لسانُ كُلِّ صديق وأجلى ما ارتسم في أذهان أولي التصوّر والتصديق؛ حَمْدُ اللهِ، مَنْ تَمسَّكَ بحججِهِ؛ أنتجت قضاياه اليقين، وحازَ قياسُهُ لِلكُلِّيَّاتِ والجزئيات الفضل المبين.
والصَّلاة والسَّلامُ على أشرف أنواع المخلوقين، الَّذِي خَتَمْتَ بِهِ النَّبِيِّين، وأَعلَيْتَ درجتَهُ في عِلّيّين، وعلى آله وأصحابِهِ الَّذين شادُوا الدِّين، واجْعَلْنَا لِهدِيهِ وهديهِمْ مُتَّبِعين، وَانْفَعْنَا بِمحبَّتِهِ وَمَحبَّةِ مَن تبعهم إلى يومٍ الدين .
أما بعد :
فيقولُ أفقرُ عبد إلى مَولاهُ القدير علي بن مصطفى المدعو بالدردير»: إنّي وجدتُ تقرير شيخ المحققين الهمامِ الشَّيخ شافعي الجناحي على شرح العلامة الخَبيصي في فنّ المنطقِ قد كتب عليهِ أُستاذنَا شيخُ الملَّةِ والدِّينِ ؛ الإمامُ العَالِمُ الشَّهِيرُ ؛ شيخُنَا وَشيخُ مَشايخنا ؛ الشَّيخُ مُحمَّد عرفة الدُّسوقي المالكي ؛ زيادات تزيدُ على النّصْفِ، وألحقها بهِ معَ تحرير بعض مواضع فيه . وكانَ غرضُهُ - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تعالى - أن يجعلَ ذلكَ حاشيةٌ مُستقلةً، فانتقل إلى جنَّاتِ النَّعيمِ، فجرَّدْتُهُ معَ ضميمة بعض تقاييد وجدتُها بهامش الشَّرحِ بخط أُستاذنَا المذكور، وسمَّيتُهُ : التَّجريد الشَّافي على تذهيب المنطقِ الكافي).
والله أسألُ أنْ ينفعَ بِهِ كَمَا نفع بأصلِهِ، إِنَّهُ على ما يشاء قدير، وبالإجابة جدير، نسأله سحانه نطقاً مؤيَّداً بالحجّةِ، وإصابةً دافعةً للمحجَّةِ، وهو حسبي ونعم الوكيل، وَلَا حولَ وَلَا قوَّةَ إِلَّا بِالله العلي العظيم.
Introduction of the Commentator
[Introduction of the Commentator]
Indeed, the sweetest logic adorned by the tongue of every friend and the most manifest what is imprinted in the minds of the people of conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) is the praise of God; whoever clings to His proofs, his propositions produce certainty, and his syllogism attains manifest excellence over universals and particulars. And prayer and peace be upon the noblest of created beings, with whom You sealed the prophets, and raised his station in the highest heavens, and upon his family and companions who supported the religion; make us followers of his guidance and theirs, and benefit us through love of him and love of those who follow them until the Day of Judgment. To proceed: The most needy servant of his Mighty Lord, Ali ibn Mustafa known as al-Dardir, says: I found the commentary (taqrir) of the chief of investigators, the energetic Shaykh Shafi' al-Janahi, on the explanation (sharh) of the eminent al-Khabisi in the art of logic; upon it our teacher, the shaykh of the religion and the milla, the famous scholar, our shaykh and the shaykh of our shaykhs, Shaykh Muhammad 'Arafa al-Dasuqi al-Maliki, had written additions exceeding half [the original], and he appended them to it along with revision of some positions in it. And his intention – may God have mercy on him – was to make that an independent gloss (hashiya), but he passed on to the gardens of bliss. So I extracted it with the inclusion of some notes (taqayid) that I found in the margin of the commentary in the handwriting of our aforementioned teacher, and I named it: al-Tajrid al-Shafi 'ala Tadhheeb al-Mantiq al-Kafi. And I ask God to benefit by it just as He benefited by its original; indeed, He is powerful over what He wills, and worthy of answering. We ask Him – exalted is He – for speech supported by proof and for rightness that clears the path; He is sufficient for me and the best of trustees, and there is no power and no strength except by God, the Most High, the Almighty.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is the preface (muqaddima) of the commentator, Ali ibn Mustafa al-Dardir, introducing his work. It opens with a rhetorical praise of logic, describing it as the sweetest discourse and the most manifest knowledge, particularly for those engaged in tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). The author states that God's proofs yield certainty and His syllogisms encompass universals and particulars, establishing the preeminence of logic. After the standard eulogy to the Prophet Muhammad and his family, the author proceeds to explain his motivation. He found a commentary (taqrir) by Shaykh Shafi' al-Janahi on the commentary (sharh) of al-Khabisi on logic. Upon this work, his own teacher, Shaykh Muhammad 'Arafa al-Dasuqi, had added substantial annotations amounting to more than half the original, along with some revisions. Al-Dasuqi intended to compile these into an independent gloss (hashiya) but passed away. Al-Dardir then extracted these annotations, supplemented them with additional notes found in the margins, and named the resulting work 'al-Tajrid al-Shafi 'ala Tadhheeb al-Mantiq al-Kafi' (The Clear Abstract on the Polishing of Sufficient Logic). Finally, he prays for divine benefit and guidance. The passage is entirely introductory and biographical, setting the stage for the logical content that follows.

Key Terms
تقرير
حاشية
شرح

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) To translate Greek logic

Key Takeaway
This preface establishes the scholarly lineage and purpose of al-Dardir's commentary, highlighting how later scholars preserve and transmit earlier contributions.

Parsing the Preface: Chain of Transmission and Purpose
Author explains his motivation and methodology for compiling the hashiah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the chain of transmission (isnad) and the author's stated purpose in a classical Islamic scholarly preface.

Core Idea
The preface establishes the hashiah's authority through a clear chain of teachers and texts.

This passage is the opening of al-Dirdir's hashiah on the logic text Tadhhib al-Mantiq. It is not a logical argument but a preface that grounds the work in the scholarly tradition. Understanding this preface allows you to trace the intellectual lineage and the compiler's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opening praise (hamdalah)
Find the phrase that praises Allah and describes logic as sweet and certain. This sets the spiritual and intellectual tone.
The author links logic to divine proofs, emphasizing its value.
2
Locate the chain of teachers
Identify the names: al-Khabisi, al-Janahi, al-Dasuqi, and al-Dirdir. Understand the relationship: al-Khabisi wrote the original commentary, al-Janahi wrote a commentary on that, al-Dasuqi added marginal notes, and al-Dirdir compiled them.
This chain (isnad) establishes the work's legitimacy.
3
Determine the author's purpose
Read the phrase 'I found that the commentary... had written upon it... additions exceeding half.' This shows al-Dirdir's aim: to compile and complete al-Dasuqi's unfinished gloss.
The phrase 'I extracted it along with the inclusion of some notes I found in the margin' explains the compilation process.
4
Note the prayer for benefit
The author asks Allah to benefit others through this work, as the original did. This is a standard supplication in Islamic prefaces.
This reinforces the work's purpose as a service to seekers of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّ أحلَى مَنطق تحلَّى بِهِ  ِلسانُ كُلِّ صديق وأجلى ما ارتسم في أذهان أولي التَّصَوُّر والتصديق؛ حَمْدُ اللهِ، مَنْ تَمسَّكَ بحججِهِ؛ أنتجت قضاياه اليقين، وحازَ قياسُهُ لِلكُلِّيَّاتِ والجزئيات الفضل المبين. والصَّلاة والسَّلامُ على أشرف أنواع المخلوقين، الَّذِي خَتَمْتَ بِهِ النَّبِيِّين، وأَعلَيْتَ درجتَهُ في عِلّيّين، وعلى آله وأصحابِهِ الَّذين شادُوا الدِّين، واجْعَلْنَا لِهدِيهِ وهديهِمْ مُتَّبِعين، وَانْفَعْنَا بِمحبَّتِهِ وَمَحبَّةِ مَن تبعهم إلى يومٍ الدين . أما بعد : فيقولُ أفقر عبد إلى مَولاهُ القدير علي بن مصطفى المدعو بالدردير»: إنّي وجدتُ تقرير شيخ المحققين الهمامِ الشَّيخ شافعي الجناحي على شرح العلامة الخَبيصي في فنّ المنطقِ قد كتب عليهِ أُستاذنَا شيخُ الملَّةِ والدِّينِ ؛ الإمامُ العَالِمُ الشَّهِيرُ ؛ شيخُنَا وَشيخُ مَشايخنا ؛ الشَّيخُ مُحمَّد عرفة الدُّسوقي المالكي ؛ زيادات تزيدُ على النّصْفِ، وألحقها بهِ معَ تحرير بعض مواضع فيه . وكانَ غرضُهُ - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تعالى - أن يجعلَ ذلكَ حاشيةٌ مُستقلةً، فانتقل إلى جنَّاتِ النَّعيمِ، فجرَّدْتُهُ معَ ضميمة بعض تقاييد وجدتُها بهامش الشَّرحِ بخط أُستاذنَا المذكور، وسمَّيتُهُ : التَّجريد الشَّافي على تذهيب المنطقِ الكافي). والله أسألُ أنْ ينفعَ بِهِ كَمَا نفع بأصلِهِ، إِنَّهُ على ما يشاء قدير، وبالإجابة جدير، نسأله سبحانه نطقاً مؤيَّداً بالحجّةِ، وإصابةً دافعةً للمحجَّةِ، وهو حسبي ونعم الوكيل، وَلَا حَوْلَ وَلَا قوَّةَ إِلَّا بِالله العلي العظيم.
Indeed, the sweetest logic adorned by the tongue of every friend and the most evident thing imprinted in the minds of those possessing conception and assent is the praise of Allah. Whoever clings to His proofs, His propositions produce certainty, and His syllogism attains manifest superiority for universals and particulars. And prayers and peace be upon the noblest of all created beings, with whom You sealed the prophets, and You raised his station in the highest ranks, and upon his family and companions who strengthened the religion; make us followers of his guidance and theirs, and benefit us through love of him and love of those who follow them until the Day of Judgment. To proceed: The poorest servant to his Mighty Lord, 'Ali ibn Mustafa known as al-Dirdir, says: I found that the commentary of the chief of the investigators, the great Shaykh Shafi'i al-Janahi, on the commentary of the distinguished al-Khabisi on the art of logic had written upon it by our teacher, the shaykh of the nation and religion, the famous learned imam, our shaykh and the shaykh of our shaykhs, Shaykh Muhammad 'Arafa al-Dasuqi al-Maliki, additions exceeding half, and he appended them to it along with revising some of its places. His purpose - may Allah have mercy on him - was to make it an independent gloss, but he passed away to the gardens of bliss. So I extracted it along with the inclusion of some notes I found in the margin of the commentary in the handwriting of our aforementioned teacher, and I named it 'al-Tajrid al-Shafi 'ala Tadhhib al-Mantiq al-Kafi'. And I ask Allah to benefit by it as He benefited by its original; indeed He is powerful over what He wills, worthy of response. We ask Him - glorified - for speech supported by proof and a correctness that beats the path. He is sufficient for us and an excellent trustee. There is no power and no strength except with Allah, the Most High, the Almighty.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is the preface (muqaddimah) of the commentator (al-muhashi) Shaykh 'Ali al-Dirdir. It begins with a poetic praise of logic, describing it as the sweetest discourse and the clearest concept for those who engage in conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The author praises Allah, whose proofs produce certainty and whose syllogism (qiyas) encompasses both universals and particulars. After the traditional salawat upon the Prophet and his family, the author states his purpose: he found that the commentary (taqrir) of Shaykh Shafi'i al-Janahi on the explanation (sharh) of al-Khabisi (presumably on the matn of Tadhhib al-Mantiq) had been annotated by his teacher, Shaykh Muhammad 'Arafa al-Dasuqi al-Maliki, with additions exceeding half the size of the original text. Al-Dasuqi also revised certain passages and intended to compile these into an independent gloss (hashiyah), but he died before completing it. Al-Dirdir then extracted these additions along with other marginal notes he found in al-Dasuqi's handwriting, and named the compilation 'al-Tajrid al-Shafi 'ala Tadhhib al-Mantiq al-Kafi'. The passage closes with a prayer for benefit, asking Allah for speech supported by proof and a correct path. This introduction is typical of Islamic scholarly works, providing the chain of transmission and the rationale for the new work.

Key Terms
حاشية (hashiyah) – gloss
تقرير (taqrir) – commentary
مقدمة (muqaddimah) – preface
إسناد (isnad) – chain of transmission

Opening Praise: The Basmalah, Hamd, and Salawat in Islamic Scholarship
Author begins the work with praise of Allah and salawat on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three-part structure of a classical Islamic scholarly opening (basmalah, hamdalah, salawat) and explain why such openings ground the work in divine authority.

Core Idea
Every Islamic scholarly work begins with praise to connect human inquiry to divine guidance.

This passage opens al-Taftazani's Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam with the standard tripartite formula: basmalah, praise of Allah, and blessings on the Prophet. For the Muslim logician, this is not mere convention—it signals that the pursuit of knowledge, including logic, is an act of worship and depends on divine grace. Even in a rigorous science like logic, the author anchors the work in the sacred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Locate the basmalah, the hamd (praise), and the salawat (blessings) in the Arabic. The basmalah is the first phrase. The hamd begins with 'فالحمد الله'. The salawat begins with 'والصلاة على رسوله'.
2
Analyze the theological basis of hamd
The author says praise opens every book and closes every answered supplication. This implies that all legitimate knowledge and answered prayers are from Allah alone. Consider how this sets the stage for a logical work.
3
Examine the role of salawat
The Prophet is called 'the firmest handhold' and 'the greatest means'. This connects the study of logic to the prophetic tradition—logic is not independent but guided by revelation.
4
Reflect on the phrase about requests to other than Allah
The phrase 'المطالب لسواه إذا رُفِعَت فهي عُقْمٌ' (requests to other than Him are barren) underscores that all true benefit comes from Allah. This reinforces tawhid and the futility of seeking knowledge without divine orientation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيْمِ تهذيب المنطقِ والكلامِ ؛ افتتاحه بالحمدِ، وتوشيحه بالشُّكرِ الَّذي بهِ النِّعَمُ تمتدُّ؛ فالحمد الله فاتحه كلّ كتاب، وخاتمة كلِّ دعاءٍ مُجَابٍ، فله الحمد فى الأُولى والآخرةِ، وله الحكم، والمطالب لسواه إذا رُفِعَت ؛ فهي عُقْمٌ، والصَّلاة على رسوله الأعظم ونبيّه الأكرم ؛ هيَ العُروةُ الوُثقَى للمُستَمسِكِينَ، والوسيلةُ العُظمى للمتوسلين، فعليهِ مِنَ اللهِ أفضل صلاة وأزكى سلام يتواليان عليه وعلى آله الفِخَامِ وصحبه الكرام.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam: its opening is with praise, and its adornment with thanksgiving through which blessings extend. So praise be to Allah, the opener of every book and the seal of every answered supplication. To Him belongs praise in the first and the last, and to Him belongs judgment. And the requests to other than Him, when raised, are barren. And blessings upon His greatest Messenger and His noblest Prophet; they are the firmest handhold for those who hold fast, and the greatest means for those who seek intercession. Upon him be from Allah the best of prayers and the purest of peace, continuously upon him and upon his noble family and his honorable companions.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the renowned work 'Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam' by Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. The author begins with the basmalah, then states that the work opens with praise and is adorned with thanks. The praise is directed to Allah, who opens every book and answers every supplication. Then comes salawat upon the Prophet Muhammad as the best intercessor. This is a standard Islamic opening for theological works.

Key Terms
بسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيْمِ
الحمد
الصلاة على النبي

Key Takeaway
A classical Islamic scholarly work opens with basmalah, hamd, and salawat to affirm that all knowledge is from Allah and that the Prophet is the means of guidance.

The Purpose of the Opening Praise
Al-Atar opens his commentary with praise of God and the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rhetorical components of a classical Islamic scholarly opening and explain their function in establishing the work's authority.

Core Idea
The opening praise is not mere decoration; it establishes the spiritual and intellectual framework of the work.

Every classical Islamic scholarly work begins with a formal opening that includes praise of God and prayers upon the Prophet. This is not just a convention—it serves to orient the reader, invoke divine blessing, and connect the work to the broader tradition. In this lesson, we examine al-Atar's opening to his commentary on Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam, a foundational text in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basmala
Locate the phrase 'بسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيْمِ' (In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful). This invocation is standard and signals that the work is undertaken with divine assistance.
The basmala is a constant feature of Islamic scholarly works, not just religious texts.
2
Find the hamdala
Look for 'الحمد لله' (Praise be to God) and note how the author describes God as the opener of every book and the seal of every answered supplication. This establishes the work's reliance on God's grace.
The hamdala often highlights specific attributes of God relevant to the work's theme.
3
Find the salawat
Identify the section beginning with 'والصَّلاة على رسوله الأعظم' (And prayers upon His greatest messenger). The Prophet is described as the firmest handhold and the greatest means, connecting the work to prophetic guidance.
The salawat reaffirms the author's adherence to the Sunna and seeks intercession through the Prophet.
4
Analyze the rhetorical function
Notice how al-Atar weaves together praise, thankfulness, and blessings. This structure serves to consecrate the work, assert its orthodoxy, and invite the reader's engagement from a place of reverence.
These openings are not merely ornamental; they frame the entire discourse within an Islamic worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيْمِ تهذيب المنطقِ والكلامِ ؛ افتتاحه بالحمدِ، وتوشيحه بالشُّكرِ الَّذي بهِ النِّعَمُ تمتدُّ؛ فالحمد الله فاتحه كلّ كتاب، وخاتمة كلِّ دعاءٍ مُجَابٍ، فله الحمد فى الأُولى والآخرةِ، وله الحكم، والمطالب لسواه إذا رُفِعَت ؛ فهي عُقْمٌ، والصَّلاة على رسوله الأعظم ونبيّه الأكرم ؛ هيَ العُروةُ الوُثقَى للمُستَمسِكِينَ، والوسيلةُ العُظمى للمتوسلين، فعليهِ مِنَ اللهِ أفضل صلاة وأزكى سلام يتواليان عليه وعلى آله الفِخَامِ وصحبه الكرام.

And requests to other than Him, when raised, are barren. And prayers upon His greatest messenger and most noble prophet; they are the firmest handhold for those who hold fast, and the greatest means for those who seek mediation. Upon him be the best prayer and purest peace from God, continually upon him and his illustrious family and his noble companions.

Commentary
This is the opening of Shaykh al-Atar's commentary on Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam. He begins by invoking God and stating that he commences with praise and adorns it with thankfulness, through which blessings extend. He praises God as the opener of every book and the seal of every accepted supplication. He then sends prayers upon the Prophet, the greatest messenger, calling him the firmest handhold and the greatest means. This is a typical rhetorical opening for a classical Islamic work.

Introduction and Front Matter

Al-Atar's Motivation for Writing
Al-Atar introduces his motivation for writing, noting the need to clarify al-Khabisi's commentary and the corruption of Yasin's hashya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Atar wrote his commentary by identifying the three texts and the problem of textual corruption.

Core Idea
Al-Atar's commentary arose from the need to correct the corrupted hashya of Shaykh Yasin on al-Khabisi's Sharh al-Tahdhib.

Al-Atar opens his commentary with a traditional 'Amma ba'd' section, where he outlines his purpose. He identifies himself humbly, then explains the existing works: al-Khabisi's concise commentary and Shaykh Yasin's gloss. However, the gloss suffered from copyist errors. This lesson helps you understand the scholarly context and the impetus for al-Atar's own work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his self-description
Read the first line: al-Atar calls himself 'al-faqir' (the poor one) and uses his full name. Note the humility and the prayer for forgiveness. This is a standard opening but it sets the tone of a sincere scholar.
Notice the epithet 'Abu al-Sa'adat' (father of blesssings).
2
Identify the three texts in the passage
Locate mentions of: 1) Sharh al-Tahdhib by al-Khabisi, 2) the hashya (gloss) by Shaykh Yasin, and 3) al-Atar's own commentary (implicitly). Understand their relationships: the hashya comments on the sharh, and al-Atar's work comments on both to clarify and correct.
3
Identify the problem that motivated al-Atar
Al-Atar states that corruption (tahrif) from copyists 'extended to' the hashya, disfiguring its virtues. This textual corruption is the key reason al-Atar felt a new commentary was needed, to restore and clarify the original intent.
Tahrif refers to both accidental and intentional alterations in manuscripts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدُ فيقولُ الفَقيرُ أبو السَّعاداتِ حسنُ بنُ مُحمَّدٍ العطَّارُ؛ غفر الله ذنوبه وستر في الدَّارَينِ عيوبه : إِنَّ شرحَ التَّهذيب للعلامةِ الخَبيصي مع وجازَةِ ألفاظه وسلاسة معانيه؛ مُحتاج إلى تَنْمِيم بعضِ مَبَاحِثَ ، وكشف غوامضَ لِمَن يُعانيه، وقد وضعَ العلامةُ الشَّيخُ ياسين عليه حاشيةً ؛ ضمَّ فيها مِن كَلِم القوم أطرافاً، وأسعف طالبيه بها إسعافاً ، بَيدَ أَنَّهُ امتدَّ إليها من أيدي النَّقَلةِ التَّحريفُ ، وشوَّهُوا محاسِنَها بكثرة
And after: The poor one, Abu al-Sa'adat Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Atar, may God forgive his sins and conceal his faults in both worlds, says: Indeed, the commentary of al-Allama al-Khabisi on Tahdhib al-Mantiq, despite the conciseness of its expressions and the smoothness of its meanings, needs elaboration of some topics and uncovering of obscurities for those who struggle with it. And al-Allama al-Shaykh Yasin wrote a gloss upon it, in which he collected parts of the discourses of the people and assisted its students with assistance. However, corruption extended to it from the hands of copyists, and they disfigured its virtues with much [error].
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This is al-Atar's 'Amma ba'd' section, where he explains his purpose. He identifies himself as Abu al-Sa'adat Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Atar, seeking God's forgiveness. He states that the commentary of al-Allama al-Khabisi on Tahdhib al-Mantiq, though concise and smooth, still requires elaboration on some topics and clarification of obscurities for those who study it. He mentions that Shaykh Yasin wrote a hashya (supercommentary) on it, which collected valuable discourses and helped students, but unfortunately copyists introduced errors (tahrif) that marred its beauty. This explains why al-Atar is writing his own commentary: to address the need for a corrected and more detailed exposition.

The Role of Supercommentaries in Islamic Scholarship
Al-Attar explains his motivation for writing this supercommentary and critiques previous works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main scholarly motivations for writing a supercommentary (hashiya) and recognize the problems that prompt such works.

Core Idea
Supercommentaries are not mere repetition; they correct errors and clarify obscurities inherited from previous layers of commentary.

In this preface, al-Attar lays out why he is writing his supercommentary on al-Khabisi's Sharh al-Tahdhib. He points to specific shortcomings: the need for supplementation of topics, obscurities that require unveiling, and textual corruption that crept into Sheikh Yasin's gloss. Understanding these motivations helps us appreciate the critical role of the hashiya tradition in preserving and refining Islamic scholarly works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's stated goal
Locate the sentence where al-Attar says that al-Khabisi's commentary 'is in need of supplementing some of its topics and unveiling its obscurities.' This is his primary motivation.
2
Recognize the problem with Sheikh Yasin's gloss
Al-Attar notes that transmitters (al-nuqala) introduced alteration (al-tahrif) and textual corruption (al-tashif) into Sheikh Yasin's hashiya. Understand that this is a critique of the chain of transmission, not the original author.
3
Distinguish the two types of criticism
Al-Attar criticizes Ibn Sa'id al-Maghribi for excessive and unfair objections. Compare this with the legitimate need for correction: al-Attar aims to produce a balanced work that avoids both uncritical acceptance and harsh rejection.
4
Connect to the broader tradition
Reflect on how this preface fits into the Islamic scholarly practice of writing supercommentaries. Each layer (matn → sharh → hashiya) aims to build on and refine the previous one.
This practice is common in logic, theology, and jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد فيقول الفقير أبو السعادات حسن بن محمد العطار غفر الله ذنوبه وستر في الدارين عيوبه : إن شرح التهذيب للعلامة الخبيصي مع وجازة ألفاظه وسلاسة معانيه محتاج إلى تنميم بعض مباحث وكشف غوامض لمن يعانيه وقد وضع العلامة الشيخ ياسين عليه حاشية ضم فيها من كلم القوم أطرافا وأسعف طالبيه بها إسعافا بيد أنه امتد إليها من أيدي النقلة التحريف وشوهوا محاسنها بكثرة التصحيف هذا مع نقله كلام الغير بدون عزم ووقوعه بمقتضى الطبع البشري في الشهو وتلاه العلامة ابن سعيد المغربي فشغف بالاعتراض عليه وولع بتعقبه في كل ما عول عليه وقد ألجأه ذلك إلى الاعتساف وتجاوز الإنصاف ووقع في أوهام وأغاليط تعكر الأفهام وقد قيل فيما سبق من الأمثال التي تناقلها الرجال قل أن سلم مكثار أو أقيل له عثار وكثيرا ما ينقل عبارة غيره موهما أنها مما سنح عندما أورى زناد فكره وقدح وربما أطال في بعض المواضع ذيل الكلام
After [this], the poor servant Abu al-Sa'adat Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Attar, may God forgive his sins and conceal his faults in the two abodes, says: Indeed, the commentary on al-Tahdhib by the scholar al-Khabisi, despite the brevity of its expressions and the smoothness of its meanings, is in need of supplementing some of its topics and unveiling its obscurities for those who undertake it. The scholar Sheikh Yasin wrote a gloss on it, gathering therein aspects of the people's discourse and aiding its students thereby with assistance. However, alteration extended to it from the hands of the transmitters, and they disfigured its beauties by much corruption. This, along with his quoting the speech of others without resolve and his falling into error by the dictate of human nature. Then the scholar Ibn Sa'id al-Maghribi followed him; he became enamored with objecting to him and was fond of tracking him in everything he relied upon. That compelled him to exaggeration and exceeding fairness, and he fell into illusions and errors that trouble understanding. It has been said in the proverbs transmitted by men: 'Rarely does a frequent talker remain safe, nor is his stumbling excused.' And he often quotes another's expression, giving the impression that it is from his own [when] he strikes the flint of his thought and strikes a spark. And perhaps he lengthens the tail of speech in some places.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is the preface written by al-Attar to his supercommentary. He begins by praising al-Khabisi's commentary on al-Tahdhib, acknowledging its conciseness and smoothness but pointing out its need for supplementation and clarification of difficult points. He then mentions Sheikh Yasin's gloss, which was valuable but was corrupted by copyists (al-nuqala) who introduced alterations and disfigured its beauties through tashif (textual corruption). Al-Attar also criticizes Sheikh Yasin for quoting others without proper attribution and for human error. Then he mentions Ibn Sa'id al-Maghribi, who followed Sheikh Yasin and became excessively critical, objecting to everything and falling into exaggeration and injustice, resulting in errors that confuse understanding. Al-Attar cites a proverb about the danger of excessive talk. He further criticizes Ibn Sa'id for often quoting others' words as if they were his own and for verbosity. This preface sets the stage for al-Attar's own work, which aims to correct these issues and provide a reliable commentary.

التهذيب
الخبيصي
الحاشية
التصحيف

D) He praised Sheikh Yasin excessively

Key Takeaway
Supercommentaries in the Islamic tradition often arise from the need to correct textual corruption and to balance critique with fairness.

Understanding the Role of Hashya in the Islamic Commentary Tradition
Author's preface explaining need for hashya and methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose and structure of a hashya as demonstrated by al-'Attar's preface.

Core Idea
The hashya completes what earlier commentaries left unfinished, like a teacher clarifying a dense lesson.

Al-'Attar's preface reveals the scholarly ethic of completing and clarifying prior works without claiming superiority. In this lesson, we'll dissect his motivations and methodology to understand how hashya function as collaborative scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Find where al-'Attar describes the difficulty caused by earlier commentaries (e.g., paths became difficult, the book became needy).
This sets the stage for why a hashya is needed.
2
Locate His Purpose
Look for the phrase 'fawaḍaʿtu hādhihi al-ḥāshiyah' and what follows: aiding students, showing compassion.
His purpose is explicitly pedagogical.
3
Analyze His Method
Identify the three actions: collecting jewels of transmissions, summarizing, clarifying what was hidden, and completing omitted material.
Note the emphasis on attributing quotes to sources ('biʿazwihi ilā qāʾilihī').
4
Understand His Attitude
Find his acknowledgment of the earlier scholars' virtue and his own admission of inability to compete.
This shows humility and respect, not criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار: معَ عدمِ ملائمةِ الحال واقتضاء المقامِ ؛ فتوغَرَت بما ارتكباه للطَّالب المسالك، وتعشرت عليه المدارك، وصار الكتاب بسبب ذلك لغيرهما محتاجاً، ومُفتقراً لِمَن يسلك سبيل العدالة منهاجاً ، فوضعْتُ هذهِ الحاشية؛ إسعافاً للطالبين، وإشفاقاً على المشتغلين، مُتَجنّباً طَرَفَي التَّفريط والإفراط، ناظماً ما التقطته من جواهر من جواهرِ النُّقولِ في أسماط، مُلخّصاً من الحاشِيَتَيْنِ مَا صَفا، مُوضحاً ما تركاه مستوراً بذيل الخفا، وما نقلاه عن الغَيرِ ؛ فإليه أرجعُ ، ومنه أستمدُّ وأتَّبع ، مُنَبِّهاً بعزوِهِ إلى قائِلِهِ؛ على أَنَّهُما منه أخذاه، وأبهما طريقَ مَغزاه، وربَّما حَذَفَا من الكلام ما تتم به فائدته، وتعظمُ عائدته، فأذكره تتميماً للكلام وتوضيحاً للمقامِ، مُعَوِّلاً في النُّقول على ما مقبول عند علماء المعقول هذا معَ اعترافي بفضل سبقهما ، وبُعْدِ شاوِهِما، معَ قُصوري عن الجري معهما في ميدان وعجزي عن مزاحمتهما في هذا الشَّان : هو
Al-'Attar: With the lack of suitability of the situation and the demand of the context, so the paths became difficult for the student due to what they committed, and the paths became complicated for him, and the book because of that became in need of others, and dependent on one who follows the path of justice as a method. So I wrote this hashya; aiding the students and showing compassion to the diligent, avoiding the extremes of negligence and excess, arranging what I picked from the jewels of jewels of transmissions in strands, summarizing from the two hashya's what is clear, clarifying what they left hidden under the tail of concealment, and what they transmitted from others; to him I refer, and from him I seek aid and follow, alerting by attributing it to its speaker; that they took it from him, and they obscured the path of its meaning, and perhaps they omitted from the speech what completes its benefit and increases its return; so I mention it to complete the speech and clarify the context, relying in transmissions on what is accepted among the scholars of rational sciences; this, with my admission of the virtue of their precedence and the distance of their level, with my inability to run with them in the field and my incapacity to compete with them in this matter: he is.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a preface from the commentator al-'Attar, explaining the need for his hashya. He says that the two earlier commentators (likely al-Dasuqi and another) made some things difficult for students, and their book requires further clarification. He wrote this hashya to aid students, avoiding extremes, and collected gems from transmissions, summarizing from the two hashya's, clarifying what they left obscure. He acknowledges that many of his points are taken from the earlier commentators, but he adds completions and clarifications. He admits the superiority of the earlier scholars.

النقول

Textual Criticism and Tawakkul in Islamic Scholarship
Al-Attar expresses reliance on God and al-Sharnubi corrects a textual error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a textual interpolation in an Islamic manuscript and explain why it corrupts the meaning.

Core Idea
Even a single extra word can undermine the textual integrity and theological precision.

This short passage from al-Attar's commentary reveals two essential pillars of Islamic scholarship: deep reliance on God (tawakkul) and meticulous textual criticism (tahqiq). Al-Attar humbly acknowledges his weakness and seeks divine grace, while al-Sharnubi adds a critical footnote correcting a scribal error. Together, they model the balance between spiritual devotion and intellectual rigour that defines the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dua and the note
The passage has two distinct parts: al-Attar's prayer (from 'العطار' to 'نعم الوكيل') and al-Sharnubi's textual correction in the footnote. Clearly separate them.
2
Analyze the textual correction
Al-Sharnubi states that in some copies there is an extra word 'min' (من) which should be deleted. Consider where 'min' appears and how it affects the Arabic construction and meaning.
The extra 'min' likely appears before 'على' in 'عوّلتُ على فيض ربي', changing the phrase from 'I relied on' to 'I took from', which alters the theological nuance.
3
Evaluate why it corrupts the meaning
Explain that adding 'min' introduces a notion of extraction rather than full reliance, undermining the concept of tawakkul. The correction preserves the intended meaning of complete dependence on God.
4
Generalize to Islamic textual studies
This correction exemplifies 'tahqiq' (critical editing). Recognize that even established texts may contain scribal errors requiring careful scrutiny. This skill is vital in hadith criticism, where a single word can change a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار وابنُ اللَّبونِ إذا مالذَّ في قَرَنٍ لم يستطعْ صَولَةَ البُزلِ القناعِيسِ لكنَّني على فَيضِ ربِّي الذي أمدَّهُما عَوَّلْتُ، ومنه استمدَّيْتُ العناية وعليهِ توكَّلْتُ، ضارعاً إليهِ بذُلِّ المسكنة والافتقار واقفاً بِبابِ إحسانِهِ الَّذي لا يُذادُ عنهُ فاجر ولا بار، سائلاً منه الإخلاص والقبول، طالباً منه النَّفعَ لكلِّ طالب بها مشغول، وهو حسبي ونعم الوكيل . (۱) كذا بالنسخ التي بأيدينا بزيادة من والصواب حذفها لإفسادها المعنى كما لا يخفى. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Al-Attar: 'When the playful camel seeks elevation, it cannot withstand the charge of the large, steadfast camels. But I rely on the overflow of my Lord, which provided them both, and from Him I seek care, and upon Him I rely, humbly supplicating with lowliness and need, standing at the door of His beneficence which no sinner or righteous person is driven away from, asking Him for sincerity and acceptance, seeking benefit for every seeker occupied with it, and He is sufficient for me and the best trustee.' (1) 'Thus in the copies we have with an extra 'min', and the correct is to delete it because it corrupts the meaning, as is not hidden.' End. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a personal note by al-Attar, expressing his humility and reliance on God's grace in his scholarly work. He compares himself to a weak camel unable to compete with strong ones, but he depends on God's overflow. He then prays for sincerity and acceptance. Following this, al-Sharnubi adds a textual note: in some copies of the text, there is an extra word 'min' (from) which should be deleted because it corrupts the meaning. This shows the careful attention to textual accuracy in the tradition.
