The Rhetorical Purpose of 'Inna' in the Hamdalah
Commentator defends the use of 'inna' in the hamdalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rhetorical function of 'inna' in the opening hamdalah of a classical Islamic text and defend its use against common objections.

Core Idea
'Inna' in the hamdalah is a rhetorical tool to capture attention, not just emphasis.

In classical Islamic texts, authors often begin with the hamdalah (praise of Allah). Yet, because it is so familiar, readers may not pay full attention. The commentator al-Attar explains why the author uses 'inna' before the hamdalah: it creates a sense of novelty and directs the listener's mind to the pending subject. This lesson unpacks the two main objections to this usage and their answers, revealing the rhetorical depth of seemingly simple phrases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections to using 'inna'
Find in the passage the two criticisms: (1) 'inna' is for emphasis but there is no doubt or denial in praising Allah; (2) the formula 'inna al-hamda lillah' does not actually express praise but only a statement about praise.
2
Analyze the first answer: broader uses of 'inna'
Al-Attar counters that 'inna' can serve other purposes, such as alerting to the high status of the report, the speaker's sincerity, or for embellishment. Check the text for these alternatives.
3
Analyze the second answer: metonymy (kinaya)
The commentator argues that praising praise (i.e., saying 'praise belongs to Allah') is itself praise because it implies Allah is worthy of praise. This is a metonymy, which is more emphatic than explicit statement. Verify this reasoning in the passage.
4
Consider the alternative: praise via basmalah
Al-Attar also notes that the basmalah at the beginning already includes praise, so the hamdalah formula reinforces it. This shows the layered rhetorical construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يَتَزيَّنُ بِنَشْرِهِ منطقُ

الدسوقي

(قَوْلُهُ : مَا ) أي : ألفاظ، فَ: «ما» نكرة، وجمله (يَتَزَيَّنُ) صفةٌ لَها، ويصح جعل ما موصولةٌ ، والجملة بعدها صِلَةٌ، والمعنى : إنَّ أحسن الكلام الذي يتزيَّنُ ... إلخ حمدُ اللهِ ; أي: الثَّناءُ عليه، وقال شيخُنَا : أي: ثناء، وقوله: (يَتزيَّن); أي: يتحسَنُ ، وقولُهُ: (بنشرِهِ); أي: رائحتِهِ; أَيْ : إِنَّ أَوْلَى ثناء ... إلخ ثناء الله . (قَوْلُهُ : مَنْطِقُ ) أي : مكانُ النُّطْقِ، وهو اللّسانُ، وهو فاعل يتزيَّن، وَلَا يخفى ما في إثباتِ الرَّائحة للكلامِ مِن الاستعارة; حيثُ شبَّهَ الثَّناءَ بِشيءٍ طَيِّبِ الرَّائحةِ كالمشكِ على طريق المكنيّة .

العطار

ولأنَّ تصدير الكُتُبِ بجملةِ الحَمْدَلَةِ من أوَّلِ الأمر شائع مألوف; فليس للنَّفس إليهِ التفات كمالها عند ما هو مُستَحدثُ لها ; إذ المستحدث يحصلُ للنَّفس إليه التفاتُ ونشاطٌ واستلذاذ، كما قيلَ: «لكلِّ جديدٍ لَذَّةٌ»، فهو نظيرُ ما قِيلَ في نكتة الالتفاتِ في الكلام، فإذا أُوردَ الكلام على هذهِ الصُّورة; أقبلَ السَّامِعُ بِكُلِّيَّتِهِ لانتظارِ المحكوم عليه، فيحصل به فضل تمكَّنَ في النَّفس، وقد نحا هذا المنحى كثير من الأدباء في رسائلهم، وأما ما أُورِدَ على مثلِه مِن أنَّ المقامَ ليس مقامَ شَكٍّ ولا إنكارٍ; حَتَّى يؤكَّدَ الحكم بـ: «إنَّ»، وأنَّ البداءة بالحمدِ المطلوب ليس حاصلاً للمؤلّف; لأنَّ هذهِ الصيغة ليست مؤدّية للحمد ، إذ المستفاد منها حكم من أحكام الحمد; فقد أُجِيبَ عن الأَوَّلِ بمنعِ انحصارِ مجيء إنَّ للتأكيد، بل قد يُؤتى بها لغير ذلك، كالتنبيه على أنَّ الخبر بلغَ في رفعةِ الشَّانِ إلى أن لا يقبل غير مؤكَّد، أو للتّنبيه على أنَّ المتكلم بالخبر على صدقٍ رغبةٍ وَوُفورِ نشاط فيه، أو للتّحسين أو لغير ذلك، وعن الثَّاني ; بأنَّ الثَّناءَ على الحمدٍ حَمْدٌ ; لأنَّه إنَّما استحقَّ هذه الصِّفاتِ من حيثُ إضافته إلى الله تعالى، الثَّناءُ على اللهِ بأَنَّهُ ذو الحمد الموصوف بما ذكر، فقد أفادت هذه الصيغة الثَّناءَ بطريقِ اللُّزوم ; فتكون كنايةٌ، وهي أبلغ من الصَّريح، أو أنَّ الحمد حصل بالبسملة; لتضمينها للثناء، وما قِيلَ مِن أنَّ الإتيان :: «إِنَّ» للتنبيه على تواضع المتكلّم واستحقار نفسه من حيث اعتقادُهُ عدم قبولِ ما يتكلَّمُ به ; ولو كان من المسلمات، أو فيقتضي
What is adorned by its spreading is the place of speech.

Al-Dasuqi:

(His statement: 'ma') means: words. So 'ma' is indefinite, and the sentence 'yatazayyanu' is an adjective for it. It is also possible to make 'ma' a relative pronoun, and the clause after it is the relative clause. The meaning: 'The best speech that is adorned... etc. is the praise of Allah' i.e., extolling Him. And our shaykh said: i.e., praise. And his statement: 'yatazayyanu' means: is beautified. And his statement: 'binashrihi' means: by its fragrance. I.e., 'Indeed the most worthy praise... etc. is praise of Allah.' (His statement: 'mantiq') means: the place of speech, which is the tongue, and it is the subject of 'yatazayyanu'. And it is not hidden what is in attributing fragrance to speech of metaphor, for he likened praise to a thing with a pleasant fragrance, like musk, in the style of implied metaphor (makniyya).

Al-Attar:

And because the beginning of books with the hamdalah from the very beginning is common and familiar; so the soul does not have attention to it as it does to what is novel to it; for the novel gives the soul attention, activity, and delight, as it is said: 'Every new thing has pleasure.' So it is analogous to what is said about the technique of attention (iltifat) in speech. Thus, when speech is presented in this form, the listener turns completely to await the subject of the judgment, and thereby extra firmness in the soul is achieved. Many literary men have followed this approach in their epistles. As for what is objected to such usage: that the context is not one of doubt or denial so as to need emphasis with 'inna', and that the beginning with the required praise is not achieved by the author because this formula does not convey praise, since what is understood from it is a ruling about praise; it has been answered to the first objection by denying that 'inna' is restricted to emphasis; rather it may be used for other purposes, such as alerting that the report has reached such a high station that it cannot be accepted without emphasis, or to alert that the speaker is truthful, desirous, and full of enthusiasm in it, or for embellishment, or for other reasons. As for the second objection, it is answered that praising praise is itself praise, because it only deserves these attributes due to its attribution to Allah; praising Allah for being the possessor of praise described as such; thus this formula conveys praise by way of implication (luzum), so it is a metonymy (kinaya), which is more emphatic than explicit statement. Alternatively, praise is achieved by the basmalah because it includes praise. And what is said that the use of 'inna' is to alert to the humility of the speaker and his self-abasement due to his belief that what he says is not acceptable, even if it is among self-evident matters, or it necessitates...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening phrase of the book. It begins by explaining the grammatical function of 'ma' in the phrase 'ma yatazayyanu binashrihi mantiq'. Al-Dasuqi states that 'ma' refers to words, and the sentence 'yatazayyanu' is an adjective for it, or 'ma' can be a relative pronoun. The meaning is that the most beautiful speech that is adorned by its fragrance is the praise of Allah. Then he explains 'yatazayyanu' as 'beautified' and 'binashrihi' as 'by its fragrance'. He then defines 'mantiq' as the place of speech (the tongue) and notes the metaphor: attributing fragrance to speech, likening praise to a pleasant-smelling thing like musk.

Then Al-Attar's commentary follows, discussing why the author began with the hamdalah before the main topic. He says it is common to begin books with the hamdalah, but because it is familiar, the soul does not pay attention to it. So the author uses the structure 'inna' to create novelty and attract the listener's attention, analogous to the technique of iltifat (sudden shift) in rhetoric. He then addresses two objections to the use of 'inna' here: (1) that 'inna' is for emphasis, but this context has no doubt or denial, and (2) that the formula does not actually express praise. The answer to the first is that 'inna' has other uses beyond emphasis, such as alerting to the high status of the report or the speaker's sincerity. The answer to the second is that praising praise is itself praise; describing praise as being of Allah is a metonymy that conveys praise by implication. Alternatively, praise is achieved through the basmalah. The text ends mid-sentence discussing another possible reason for using 'inna' (to show humility), but it cuts off.

منطق
يتزين
نشر

Checkpoint
According to al-Attar, why did the author begin with the hamdalah using 'inna'?
A) To emphasize a doubted statement
B) To attract the listener's attention by creating a sense of novelty
C) To indicate that the speech is not acceptable
D) To fulfill the requirement of praise

Key Takeaway
The particle 'inna' in the hamdalah serves a rhetorical purpose beyond emphasis: it captures the listener's attention by introducing the familiar in a novel form, and the resulting praise is conveyed through metonymy or implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the opening of any classical Islamic text (e.g., the Muqaddimah of Ibn Khaldun). Identify whether 'inna' is used before the hamdalah and explain, based on al-Attar's arguments, why the author might have chosen this form.
?
Write a short paragraph in Arabic that begins with 'إن الحمد لله' and then add a sentence that justifies your use of 'inna' by referencing either attention-grabbing or humility, as discussed in the passage.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Interpreting Al-Qasi and Al-Hadir: Rhetorical and Theological Dimensions
Commentary explains the meaning of 'al-qasi' and 'al-hadir' in the author's introduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate multiple interpretations of a commentator's key terms by analyzing their rhetorical and theological layers.

Core Idea
The meanings of 'al-qasi' and 'al-hadir' unfold through layered rhetorical analysis.

In the opening of a logic text, the commentator al-Dasuqi unpacks the words 'al-qasi' and 'al-hadir' using rhetorical devices like kenning (makniyya) and imaginative speech (takhyeel). This lesson shows how such analysis prevents literal misreading and reveals the author's intent in praising Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meanings
First, note the basic meanings: 'al-qasi' means 'the distant one' and 'al-hadir' means 'the present one'. The commentator starts by explaining these as spatial distance from the author.
These literal meanings serve as the foundation for further layers.
2
Recognize the metaphorical extension
The commentator introduces a second interpretation: 'al-qasi' as the disbeliever (distant from Allah's mercy) and 'al-hadir' as the one close to the perfumer. This shifts from physical to spiritual distance, employing a kenning where words are likened to fragrant substances.
The metaphor is supported by 'tarshih' (tracing back) and 'takhyeel' (imaginative extension).
3
Assess the theological usage
Finally, evaluate why the commentator considers the disbeliever interpretation remote: it would inappropriately include the disbeliever with the Muslim in the order of praise. This shows that rhetorical analysis must align with theological appropriateness.
The commentator uses this to justify the primary interpretation and reject objections.

ألفاظ، والمنطقُ اسمُ مكانٍ ؛ أي: محلَّ النُّطْقِ، أو مصدر ميمي بمعنى النُّطق، و(القاصي) البعيد، والدَّاني القريب، والمقصود تعميم الأفراد، وفي الكلام مكنيَّةٌ ؛ بتشبيه الألفاظ بشيءٍ ذي ريح ، وإثباتُ النَّشرِ الَّذي هو الرائحةُ الطَّيِّبَةُ ؛ تخييل على أحدِ المذاهب في المكنيَّةِ والتَّخييليَّة، والمعنى : إنَّ أحقِّ ألفاظ يتزيَّنُ برائحتها الطَّيِّبة محلُّها . إلخ، ومنه ظهرَ أنَّ الأولى يتعطَّرُ بدلَ يتزيَّنُ ؛ لأَنَّهُ المناسب لتشبيه الألفاظ بذي الرِّيحِ الطَّيِّبِ، وأنَّ المراد من المنطق؛ الاحتمالُ الأَوَّلُ، فَإِنْ أُريدَ الثَّاني ؛ فالمعنى : إِنَّ أَحقِّ ألفاظ يتزيَّنُ برائحتها الطَّيِّبة؛ منشؤها ومبدؤها الصَّادرة هى عنه، التَّلقَّظُ ؛ أعني : المعنى المصدري المفسَّرَ بهِ المنطقُ ، ولَمَّا كانَ هذا الاحتمال وهو خفيّا ؛ سلك أرباب الحواشي الأوَّل، وجعل «ما» واقعةً على ألفاظ ؛ إنَّ الألفاظ قوالب المعاني؛ تخيل من حيثُ إِنَّ المعنى يُفهم عند سماعِ اللفظ ، وإلا ؛ فَمَحلُّ المعاني هو النَّفْسُ النَّاطقة وحدها ؛ أي: هو وَقُواها على خلافٍ في ذلك، وكذا تفسير القاصي بغير المنعم غيرُ منعم عليه، ووجه البُعدِ : أَنَّ إرادة الكافرِ في أمثال هذهِ المقاماتِ وسَلْكَهُ مع المسلم في هذا النّظامِ؛ مِمَّا يأباه كلَّ عاقل، فضلاً عن فاضل . (قَوْلُهُ : ويَتَوَشَحُ بِذِكْرِهِ ... إلخ) عطفٌ على يتزيَّنُ ؛ عطفُ صلةٍ على صلة، أو صفة على صفة على احتمالي ما ، والتَّوشيحُ : لبس الوشاح، وهو أديم عريضٌ مُرصّع بالجواهر، وتجعله المرأة بين عاتقها وكَشْحِها ، والصُّدور : جمع صدر، وهو محلّ القلبِ مِنَ الإنسان، وهو أوَّلُ كلِّ شيءٍ، والكتبُ : جمع كتاب، والدفاتر : جمعُ دفتر، وكسر داله لغة، وهو جريدة الحسابِ والمراد بها هنا : الرَّسائلُ الصَّغيرة، عبر عنها بالدَّفترِ لما أنَّ كُلًّا يُتَذَكَّرُ بِهِ ما اشتمل عليه، والداعي للتعبير بها دونَ
Introduction of the commentator. Al-qasi and al-hadir. Al-Dasuqi. And the establishing of the spreading is imaginative, and it is adorned with tracing back; either remaining on its real meaning, or borrowed to make it fragrant, or that it is imaginative, and his statement: 'by its spreading' is tracing back. His statement: 'al-qasi' means the distant from the author, and 'al-hadir' means present with him. It is also possible that what is meant by 'al-qasi' is the distant from the mercy of God, that is the disbeliever, and what is meant by 'al-hadir' is the close to the perfumer, in order to refute those who deny its content based on denying the Creator, and that the existence of the world is accidental, or to refute those who deny that and say: what is more deserving is the basmalah or takbir or tasbih, and the like, which are cold pretensions. As for the first, because denying self-evident truths is stubbornness, so no attention is paid to refuting its denier. As for the second, because the one who says that the temporal origination of the world is accidental is outside the realm of rational people, so no attention is paid to the likes of him, like the sophists. Therefore, none of the theologians paid attention to mentioning their creeds and refuting them, unlike the rest of the sects. As for the third, because no scholar said that what is required to begin with is something other than praise. And 'ma' is a relative pronoun or an indefinite noun referring to words, and 'al-mantiq' is a noun of place meaning the place of speech, or a verbal noun meaning speech. And 'al-qasi' is the distant, and 'al-dani' is the near, and the intended meaning is generalization of individuals. And in the speech there is a kenning, by likening words to something having a scent, and establishing the spread which is the good fragrance is imaginative according to one of the schools in kenning and imaginative speech. And the meaning: indeed the most deserving of words that are adorned with its good fragrance is its place, etc. From this it appears that 'yata'ttaru' (perfumes) is more appropriate than 'yatazayyanu' (adorns), because it is suitable for the likening of words to something with a good scent, and that what is meant by 'al-mantiq' is the first possibility. If the second is intended, then the meaning: indeed the most deserving of words that are adorned with its good fragrance are their origin and source from which they issue, which is 'al-talfuz' (articulation), meaning the verbal meaning by which 'al-mantiq' is interpreted. And since this possibility is subtle, the commentators adopted the first, and made 'ma' refer to words; this is in accordance with reality, because His praise is of the category of words, and because of the expression 'mantiq'. And the mention of the possibility that it refers to meanings or inscriptions, as was said, is extremely remote, because meanings are not uttered nor inscribed. Their saying that words are the molds of meanings is imaginative in that the meaning is understood when hearing the word, otherwise the locus of meanings is the rational soul alone, that is, it and its faculties, with a difference of opinion on that. Likewise, the interpretation of 'al-qasi' as the one not favored with blessings, and 'al-dani' as the one favored. And when they interpreted it thus, they sensed the occurrence of a question: that the blessings of God Almighty are general for all His creation. So they repelled it by interpreting 'blessing' as what is suitable and ends with praise, so the disbeliever in this sense is not favored. And the aspect of remoteness is that intending the disbeliever in such contexts and including him with the Muslim in this order is rejected by every rational person, let alone a virtuous one. His statement: 'and it is adorned with his mention... etc.' is a conjunction to 'yattazzaynu', connecting a relative clause to a relative clause, or an adjective to an adjective according to the two possibilities. And 'al-tawshihu' is the wearing of the sash, which is a wide piece of leather studded with jewels, and the woman places it between her shoulder and her side. And 'al-sudur' is the plural of 'sadr', which is the place of the heart in a human, and it is the beginning of everything. And 'al-kutub' is the plural of 'kitab', and 'al-dafatir' is the plural of 'daftar', and its daal may be broken, and it is a ledger, and what is meant here is small epistles, so called because each is remembered by what it contains, and the reason for expressing with them instead of
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening phrase of the matn, specifically explaining the words 'al-qasi' and 'al-hadir'. The commentator begins by giving multiple interpretations. The first interpretation: 'al-qasi' means distant from the author, 'al-hadir' means present with the author. The second interpretation: 'al-qasi' refers to the disbeliever (distant from Allah's mercy), and 'al-hadir' refers to the one close to the perfumer, with the purpose of refuting those who deny the content of the praise based on atheism or who suggest a different opening. The commentator then discusses three types of objections and why they are invalid: (1) Denying self-evident truths is mere stubbornness, not worth refuting. (2) Claiming the world is accidental is sophistry. (3) No scholar has claimed that anything other than praise should begin with. The commentator then analyzes the syntax: 'ma' is a relative pronoun referring to words, 'al-mantiq' is either a noun of place (the place of speech) or a verbal noun. The phrase involves a kenning (makniyya), likening words to something fragrant, and 'ithbat al-nashr' is imaginative. The intended meaning is that the most deserving words to be adorned with the good fragrance are those of praise. The commentator notes that 'yata'ttaru' (perfumes) is more appropriate than 'yatazayyanu' (adorns) for the metaphor. He then discusses the interpretation of 'al-mantiq' and rejects the possibility of 'ma' referring to meanings or inscriptions, as meanings are not uttered. The commentary also addresses a potential question about whether blessings are universal, answering that 'blessing' here means what is suitable and ends with praise, so disbelievers are not included. Finally, the commentator explains 'tawshih' (wearing a sash) as a metaphor for adornment, and defines 'sudur', 'kutub', and 'dafatir' as words for books and notebooks.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's analysis shows that understanding a term in a logical text requires considering both literal and metaphorical layers along with theological appropriateness.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
The Rhetorical Layers of 'Yutawashshahu'
Explaining the figurative use of 'يتوشح'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between majaz mursal (metonymy) and isti'ara taba'iyya (derivative metaphor) by applying the criteria discussed to any given Arabic expression.

Core Idea
One verb, two rhetorical derivations: the sash is the cause of adornment, but language can borrow its name.

The commentator unpacks the verb 'yutawashshahu' used in the opening praise. He explains that it is a synonym of 'yatazayyanu' and then analyzes its rhetorical origin. This analysis is not mere philology—it trains you to see how Arabic constructs meaning through cause-effect relations and similarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base word
Find 'tawshih' and its literal meaning: wearing the wishaah (a broad, jeweled sash).
The sash is a literal object worn by women.
2
Understand the effect
Wearing the sash causes adornment—the effect is being adorned.
The cause is the action of donning the sash.
3
Check the relation: cause or similarity?
If the relation is causal (cause stands for effect), it's majaz mursal. If it's based on resemblance (the action of wearing is like adorning), it's isti'ara taba'iyya.
The word 'yutawashshahu' can be analyzed both ways.
4
Decide based on criteria
Apply the criterion: if the meaning is 'adorn' and the original term refers to wearing a sash, then using the verb for adornment is either metonymy (cause for effect) or metaphor (likening adornment to wearing).
The commentator leaves both possibilities open.

ويُتوشّحُ بذِكرِهِ خلقة. الدسوقي رحمة الله، وهو المؤمن، وحينئذ فالمعنى : إن أحسن الكلام الذي يتزيَّنُ برائحته لسان المؤمن والكافر أو الشَّخص القريب والبعيد؛ الثناء على الله، أو إن أشرف ألفاظ يتزيَّنُ بِرائحَتِهَا لسانُ الكافر والمؤمن، أو الشخص القريب والبعيد؛ الثناء على الله ، أو إنَّ أشرف ألفاظ يتزيَّنُ بِرائحَتِهَا لِسانُ الكافر والمؤمن أو الشخص القريب من المصنّفِ، والبعيد منه ؛ الثَّناء على الله، وعلى الاحتمالين فهذا كنايةٌ عَنْ تعميم الأفراد، فالمراد : إنَّ أحسن الكلام الذي يتزيَّنُ بِرائحَتِهِ لسانُ كُلّ أحدٍ : ثناء الله . ؛ (قَوْلُهُ : وَيُتَوَشَّحُ بِذِكْرِهِ) أَيْ : بذِكْرِ مَا ؛ أي: الألفاظ، وهو عطف على يتزين مرادف له والجملة محتملة؛ لكونها صفةٌ أو صلةٌ ؛ لأنَّ المعطوف على المحتمل لذلك محتمل له، ومعنى يتوشَحُ : يتزيَّنُ إمَّا على جهةِ المجاز المرسل حيثُ أُطلقَ اسمُ السَّببِ وهو التَّوشيحُ على المسبِّبِ وهو التَّزيُّن، أو على جهة الاستعارة التَّبعيَّةِ؛ حيثُ شُبّه فيه قلبُ التَّوشيح ؛ أي: إلباس الوشاح بالتَّزينِ، واستُعير اسم المشبه به للمشبَّه، واشتُقَّ مِنَ التَّوشيح يتوشَّحُ بمعنى يَتزيَّن، والتَّوشيحُ في الأصل إلباس الوشاح، وهو شيءٌ يُتَّخذُ مِن أديم؛ أي: جِلْدِ عريض، ويُرصع بالجواهر، تجعله المرأة بين عاتقها وخاصرتها ؛ بأن تلبسه كلبس السَّيف، والخاصرة: ما لانَ من الجانب، والعاتق : المنكبُ (قَوْلُهُ: صُدُوْرُ جمعُ صدر ؛ محلُّ القلب، فيكون في الكتب والدفاتر، استعارة بالكناية؛ حيثُ شبههما بالنِّساءِ الحِسَانِ بجامع الحُسْنِ والشَّرفِ، وصدورُ تَخييل، ويتوشّح ترشيح ، وشبه حمد الله بالوشاح على طريق المكنية، والتوشيح تخييل، ويحتمل أن يُراد بصدورِ الكُتُبِ : أوائِلُها، فيكون شبه أوائل الكتب بالنِّساء على طريق المكنية، ويتوشَّحُ تخييل (قَوْلُهُ : الْكُتُبِ جمعُ كتاب، وهو الصَّحيفةُ والدَّفاتر جمع دفتر، وهو جريدة الحساب ؛ أي: الورقُ الَّذي يكتب فيه الحساب بينَ النَّاس، ولم يعرف اشتقاقُ الدَّفترِ مِن أيّ شيء (قَوْلُهُ : حَمْدُ اللهِ) خبر «إِنَّ» ؛ أي : ثناؤه . (قَوْلُهُ : جَلَّ جَلالُهُ من باب الإخبارِ ؛ أي: عَظُمَتْ عَظَمتُهُ؛ أَيْ: تَنَزَّهَتْ عَظَمَتُهُ عَنِ النَّقائص، أو إنَّه إنشاء لإظهار ذلك.

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى آلَائِهِ مُتعلّق بحمد، وَمَا بينَهما ،مُعترض، قصد به التنزيه، وهو جمع إلى بالقصر، وهو النعمة، فالهمزة الأولى همزةُ الجمع، والثانية فاء المفردِ

العطار

الرَّسائل موافقةُ السَّجع، وحملها على المعنى الحقيقي كما قالوا بعيد في هذا المقام؛ إذ الدَّفاتر ليست من الأمور ذواتِ البالِ الَّتي تُصدَّرُ بالحمدِ ، بل كثيراً ما يُذكَرُ فيها ما ينزّه الحمد عن أن يصدرَ بهِ فيها ؛ كدفاتر المظالم والمعاملاتِ، ويَتَوَشَّحُ : مجاز مُرسَلٌ تبعي أو استعارةٌ مُصرَّحةٌ تبعيَّةٌ ليَتزيَّن، علاقتَهُ السَّببيَّةُ أو المشابهة أو استعارة تخييلية للمكنية في قوله : (بذكره) بتشبيه الذكرِ بالوشاح، ثمَّ إِنْ أُريدَ مِن صُدورِ الكتب أوائِلُها ؛ فالكلام على حقيقته، وإنْ أُريد بها محلُّ القَلبِ؛ فالإضافةُ مِن قبيل إضافة المشبه به للمشبه بجامع الاشتمال على كلِّ نفيس، أو الكتب استعارة تخييلية، لتشبيهها بإنسان له صدرُ ، والصدور تخييل، ويتوشَّحُ ترشيح .
And it is adorned by His mention. [The text: the meaning is: Indeed, the best speech that is adorned by its fragrance is the tongue of the believer and the disbeliever, or the near and far person; praise of God. Or: Indeed, the most honorable words that are adorned by their fragrance are the tongue of the disbeliever and the believer, or the near and far person; praise of God. Or: Indeed, the most honorable words that are adorned by their fragrance are the tongue of the disbeliever and the believer, or the person near to the author and far from him; praise of God. And upon both probabilities, this is a metaphor for the generalization of individuals. So the intended meaning is: Indeed, the best speech that is adorned by its fragrance is the tongue of everyone: praise of God. (His statement: 'and it is adorned by His mention') means: by the mention of what; i.e., the words. And it is a conjunction to 'it is adorned' as a synonym, and the sentence is probable for being an adjective or a relative clause, because the conjunction to something probable is probable for it. The meaning of 'yutawashshahu' is: it adorns, either by way of metonymy (majaz mursal) where the name of the cause, which is 'tawshih', is applied to the effect, which is adornment; or by way of derivative metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya), where the heart of 'tawshih' – i.e., wearing the sash – is compared to adornment, and the name of the likened-to is used for the likened, and from 'tawshih' the verb 'yutawashshahu' is derived meaning 'it adorns'. And 'tawshih' originally is the wearing of the sash (wishaah), which is something taken from broad leather, studded with jewels, which a woman places between her shoulder and her flank, wearing it like a sword. The flank is the soft part of the side, the shoulder is the collarbone. (His statement: 'sudur' is the plural of 'sadr', the place of the heart. Thus in the context of books and ledgers, it is a metaphorical metonymy (isti'ara bil-kinnaya), likening them to beautiful women by the commonality of beauty and honor, and 'sudur' is imaginative (takhyeel), and 'yutawashshahu' is confirmatory (tarshih). And he likens praise of God to the sash by way of implicit metaphor (al-makniyya), and 'tawshih' is imaginative. And it is possible that by 'sudur al-kutub' is meant their beginnings, in which case it likens the beginnings of books to women by way of implicit metaphor, and 'yutawashshahu' is imaginative. (His statement: 'al-kutub' is the plural of 'kitab', which is the leaf; and 'al-dafatir' is the plural of 'daftar', which is the account ledger; i.e., the paper on which accounts are written between people, and the derivation of 'daftar' is unknown. (His statement: 'hamd Allah' is the predicate of 'inna'; i.e., His praise. (His statement: 'jalla jalaluhu' is from the chapter of reporting; i.e., His greatness is magnified; i.e., His greatness is exalted above deficiencies, or it is a declaration to show that. (His statement: 'ala ala'ihi' is attached to 'hamd', and what is between them is a parenthetical remark intended for exaltation. And it is the plural of 'ila' with shortening, meaning blessing; the first hamza is the plural hamza, and the second is the fa' of the singular. [Marginal note: al-Attar] The letters conform to rhyme, and taking them (i.e., 'dafatir') in the literal sense, as they said, is far-fetched in this context, since ledgers are not among the important things that are begun with praise, but rather many times they contain things that preclude praise from being begun with them, like ledgers of grievances and transactions. And 'yutawashshahu' is a derivative metonymy or a derivative explicit metaphor meaning 'it adorns', its relation being causal or similarity, or an imaginative metaphor for the implicit metaphor in His statement 'by His mention' by comparing the mention to the sash. Then, if by 'sudur al-kutub' is meant their beginnings, then the speech is literal; if by it is meant the place of the heart, then the addition is of the type of adding the likened-to to the likened by the commonality of containing every precious thing, or the books are an imaginative metaphor, likening them to a human having a chest, and 'sudur' is imaginative, and 'yutawashshahu' is confirmatory.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the introductory praise of the original matn. The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'yutawashshahu bi-dhikrihi' (and it is adorned by His mention). He first considers possible interpretations of the earlier part, which states that the best speech is praise of God regardless of who utters it – believer or disbeliever, near or far. This is a kinnayah (metaphorical expression) indicating universality. Then he proceeds to the specific word 'yutawashshahu'. The commentator explains that it is a conjunction to 'yatazayyanu' (it is adorned) and is a synonym. The verb 'yutawashshahu' is derived from 'tawshih', which originally refers to wearing a 'wishaah' – a broad leather sash studded with jewels worn by women between the shoulder and flank. The commentator explains that the word is used figuratively either as a majaz mursal (metonymy) where the cause (putting on the sash) stands for the effect (adornment), or as an isti'ara taba'iyya (derivative metaphor) where the action of wearing a sash is likened to adornment, and the verb is derived from the likeness. He then describes the physical sash in detail. Next, the commentator analyzes the phrase 'sudur al-kutub wa al-dafatir' (the chests of books and ledgers). He notes that 'sudur' is the plural of 'sadr' (chest), the place of the heart. When applied to books and ledgers, it is a isti'ara bil-kinnaya (metaphorical metonymy), likening the books to beautiful women who have chests adorned with sashes. Thus 'sudur' serves as takhyeel (imaginative element) and 'yutawashshahu' as tarshih (confirmatory element). He then presents an alternative interpretation: that 'sudur' here means the beginnings (awa'il) of the books, in which case the likening is between the openings of books and women, with 'yutawashshahu' being the imaginative element. The commentator also defines 'kutub' and 'dafatir', noting that 'daftar' is a ledger for accounts, and its etymology is unknown. He then proceeds to parse the phrase 'hamd Allah' (praise of God) as the predicate of 'inna', and 'jalla jalaluhu' as a report of magnification. Finally, he remarks on the word 'ala' (blessings) and notes that the choice of 'dafatir' (ledgers) over 'rasa'il' (letters) is because ledgers are often base, so using them in the context of praise is a deliberate choice for rhetorical effect. The entire commentary serves to unpack the layered metaphorical and rhetorical features of the opening line.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the literal meaning of 'al-tawshih'?
A) Adorning oneself
B) Wearing a sash
C) Praising God
D) Writing in ledgers

Key Takeaway
The verb 'yutawashshahu' is not used literally; it is a rhetorical construction derived from the image of a sash worn for adornment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the verb 'استغفر' (istaghfara) in the phrase 'استغفر الله'. Is it used literally or rhetorically? Apply the same criteria: is the relation causal (asking forgiveness causes purification) or similarity-based (asking is like seeking protection)?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Implicit Metaphor of Books' Chests
Exploring the metaphor in 'صدور الكتب'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and parse an implicit metaphor (isti'ara bil-kinnaya) by locating its imaginative (takhyeel) and confirmatory (tarshih) elements.

Core Idea
Books are beautiful women; their 'chests' are adorned with the praise of God as a sash.

The commentator then turns to the phrase 'sudur al-kutub wa al-dafatir'. He notes that 'sudur' (chests) is plural of 'sadr', the place of the heart. By applying this to books, the author creates a layered metaphor where books are likened to women and the opening praise to a sash. This section teaches you to deconstruct such implicit metaphors in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main metaphor
Recognize that books are being compared to beautiful women, as indicated by the context of adornment.
The comparison is not explicit but implied.
2
Find the implicit element
The word 'sudur' (chests) is the clue: it implies that the books have chests, like women.
This is the imaginative element (takhyeel).
3
Locate takhyeel and tarshih
In this metaphor, 'sudur' is the takhyeel (imaginative component) and 'yutawashshahu' is the tarshih (confirmatory component) that reinforces the likening to a sash.
The commentator analyzes the metaphor as isti'ara bil-kinnaya.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
If 'sudur' is interpreted as the beginnings of books, then the metaphor shifts: the openings are likened to women adorned with praise.
Both interpretations are valid according to the commentator.

ويُتوشّحُ بذِكرِهِ خلقة. الدسوقي رحمة الله، وهو المؤمن، وحينئذ فالمعنى : إن أحسن الكلام الذي يتزيَّنُ برائحته لسان المؤمن والكافر أو الشَّخص القريب والبعيد؛ الثناء على الله، أو إن أشرف ألفاظ يتزيَّنُ بِرائحَتِهَا لسانُ الكافر والمؤمن، أو الشخص القريب والبعيد؛ الثناء على الله ، أو إنَّ أشرف ألفاظ يتزيَّنُ بِرائحَتِهَا لِسانُ الكافر والمؤمن أو الشخص القريب من المصنّفِ، والبعيد منه ؛ الثَّناء على الله، وعلى الاحتمالين فهذا كنايةٌ عَنْ تعميم الأفراد، فالمراد : إنَّ أحسن الكلام الذي يتزيَّنُ بِرائحَتِهِ لسانُ كُلّ أحدٍ : ثناء الله . ؛ (قَوْلُهُ : وَيُتَوَشَّحُ بِذِكْرِهِ) أَيْ : بذِكْرِ مَا ؛ أي: الألفاظ، وهو عطف على يتزين مرادف له والجملة محتملة؛ لكونها صفةٌ أو صلةٌ ؛ لأنَّ المعطوف على المحتمل لذلك محتمل له، ومعنى يتوشَحُ : يتزيَّنُ إمَّا على جهةِ المجاز المرسل حيثُ أُطلقَ اسمُ السَّببِ وهو التَّوشيحُ على المسبِّبِ وهو التَّزيُّن، أو على جهة الاستعارة التَّبعيَّةِ؛ حيثُ شُبّه فيه قلبُ التَّوشيح ؛ أي: إلباس الوشاح بالتَّزينِ، واستُعير اسم المشبه به للمشبَّه، واشتُقَّ مِنَ التَّوشيح يتوشَّحُ بمعنى يَتزيَّن، والتَّوشيحُ في الأصل إلباس الوشاح، وهو شيءٌ يُتَّخذُ مِن أديم؛ أي: جِلْدِ عريض، ويُرصع بالجواهر، تجعله المرأة بين عاتقها وخاصرتها ؛ بأن تلبسه كلبس السَّيف، والخاصرة: ما لانَ من الجانب، والعاتق : المنكبُ (قَوْلُهُ: صُدُوْرُ جمعُ صدر ؛ محلُّ القلب، فيكون في الكتب والدفاتر، استعارة بالكناية؛ حيثُ شبههما بالنِّساءِ الحِسَانِ بجامع الحُسْنِ والشَّرفِ، وصدورُ تَخييل، ويتوشّح ترشيح ، وشبه حمد الله بالوشاح على طريق المكنية، والتوشيح تخييل، ويحتمل أن يُراد بصدورِ الكُتُبِ : أوائِلُها، فيكون شبه أوائل الكتب بالنِّساء على طريق المكنية، ويتوشَّحُ تخييل (قَوْلُهُ : الْكُتُبِ جمعُ كتاب، وهو الصَّحيفةُ والدَّفاتر جمع دفتر، وهو جريدة الحساب ؛ أي: الورقُ الَّذي يكتب فيه الحساب بينَ النَّاس، ولم يعرف اشتقاقُ الدَّفترِ مِن أيّ شيء (قَوْلُهُ : حَمْدُ اللهِ) خبر «إِنَّ» ؛ أي : ثناؤه . (قَوْلُهُ : جَلَّ جَلالُهُ من باب الإخبارِ ؛ أي: عَظُمَتْ عَظَمتُهُ؛ أَيْ: تَنَزَّهَتْ عَظَمَتُهُ عَنِ النَّقائص، أو إنَّه إنشاء لإظهار ذلك.

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى آلَائِهِ مُتعلّق بحمد، وَمَا بينَهما ،مُعترض، قصد به التنزيه، وهو جمع إلى بالقصر، وهو النعمة، فالهمزة الأولى همزةُ الجمع، والثانية فاء المفردِ

العطار

الرَّسائل موافقةُ السَّجع، وحملها على المعنى الحقيقي كما قالوا بعيد في هذا المقام؛ إذ الدَّفاتر ليست من الأمور ذواتِ البالِ الَّتي تُصدَّرُ بالحمدِ ، بل كثيراً ما يُذكَرُ فيها ما ينزّه الحمد عن أن يصدرَ بهِ فيها ؛ كدفاتر المظالم والمعاملاتِ، ويَتَوَشَّحُ : مجاز مُرسَلٌ تبعي أو استعارةٌ مُصرَّحةٌ تبعيَّةٌ ليَتزيَّن، علاقتَهُ السَّببيَّةُ أو المشابهة أو استعارة تخييلية للمكنية في قوله : (بذكره) بتشبيه الذكرِ بالوشاح، ثمَّ إِنْ أُريدَ مِن صُدورِ الكتب أوائِلُها ؛ فالكلام على حقيقته، وإنْ أُريد بها محلُّ القَلبِ؛ فالإضافةُ مِن قبيل إضافة المشبه به للمشبه بجامع الاشتمال على كلِّ نفيس، أو الكتب استعارة تخييلية، لتشبيهها بإنسان له صدرُ ، والصدور تخييل، ويتوشَّحُ ترشيح .
And it is adorned by His mention. [The text: the meaning is: Indeed, the best speech that is adorned by its fragrance is the tongue of the believer and the disbeliever, or the near and far person; praise of God. Or: Indeed, the most honorable words that are adorned by their fragrance are the tongue of the disbeliever and the believer, or the near and far person; praise of God. Or: Indeed, the most honorable words that are adorned by their fragrance are the tongue of the disbeliever and the believer, or the person near to the author and far from him; praise of God. And upon both probabilities, this is a metaphor for the generalization of individuals. So the intended meaning is: Indeed, the best speech that is adorned by its fragrance is the tongue of everyone: praise of God. (His statement: 'and it is adorned by His mention') means: by the mention of what; i.e., the words. And it is a conjunction to 'it is adorned' as a synonym, and the sentence is probable for being an adjective or a relative clause, because the conjunction to something probable is probable for it. The meaning of 'yutawashshahu' is: it adorns, either by way of metonymy (majaz mursal) where the name of the cause, which is 'tawshih', is applied to the effect, which is adornment; or by way of derivative metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya), where the heart of 'tawshih' – i.e., wearing the sash – is compared to adornment, and the name of the likened-to is used for the likened, and from 'tawshih' the verb 'yutawashshahu' is derived meaning 'it adorns'. And 'tawshih' originally is the wearing of the sash (wishaah), which is something taken from broad leather, studded with jewels, which a woman places between her shoulder and her flank, wearing it like a sword. The flank is the soft part of the side, the shoulder is the collarbone. (His statement: 'sudur' is the plural of 'sadr', the place of the heart. Thus in the context of books and ledgers, it is a metaphorical metonymy (isti'ara bil-kinnaya), likening them to beautiful women by the commonality of beauty and honor, and 'sudur' is imaginative (takhyeel), and 'yutawashshahu' is confirmatory (tarshih). And he likens praise of God to the sash by way of implicit metaphor (al-makniyya), and 'tawshih' is imaginative. And it is possible that by 'sudur al-kutub' is meant their beginnings, in which case it likens the beginnings of books to women by way of implicit metaphor, and 'yutawashshahu' is imaginative. (His statement: 'al-kutub' is the plural of 'kitab', which is the leaf; and 'al-dafatir' is the plural of 'daftar', which is the account ledger; i.e., the paper on which accounts are written between people, and the derivation of 'daftar' is unknown. (His statement: 'hamd Allah' is the predicate of 'inna'; i.e., His praise. (His statement: 'jalla jalaluhu' is from the chapter of reporting; i.e., His greatness is magnified; i.e., His greatness is exalted above deficiencies, or it is a declaration to show that. (His statement: 'ala ala'ihi' is attached to 'hamd', and what is between them is a parenthetical remark intended for exaltation. And it is the plural of 'ila' with shortening, meaning blessing; the first hamza is the plural hamza, and the second is the fa' of the singular. [Marginal note: al-Attar] The letters conform to rhyme, and taking them (i.e., 'dafatir') in the literal sense, as they said, is far-fetched in this context, since ledgers are not among the important things that are begun with praise, but rather many times they contain things that preclude praise from being begun with them, like ledgers of grievances and transactions. And 'yutawashshahu' is a derivative metonymy or a derivative explicit metaphor meaning 'it adorns', its relation being causal or similarity, or an imaginative metaphor for the implicit metaphor in His statement 'by His mention' by comparing the mention to the sash. Then, if by 'sudur al-kutub' is meant their beginnings, then the speech is literal; if by it is meant the place of the heart, then the addition is of the type of adding the likened-to to the likened by the commonality of containing every precious thing, or the books are an imaginative metaphor, likening them to a human having a chest, and 'sudur' is imaginative, and 'yutawashshahu' is confirmatory.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the introductory praise of the original matn. The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'yutawashshahu bi-dhikrihi' (and it is adorned by His mention). He first considers possible interpretations of the earlier part, which states that the best speech is praise of God regardless of who utters it – believer or disbeliever, near or far. This is a kinnayah (metaphorical expression) indicating universality. Then he proceeds to the specific word 'yutawashshahu'. The commentator explains that it is a conjunction to 'yatazayyanu' (it is adorned) and is a synonym. The verb 'yutawashshahu' is derived from 'tawshih', which originally refers to wearing a 'wishaah' – a broad leather sash studded with jewels worn by women between the shoulder and flank. The commentator explains that the word is used figuratively either as a majaz mursal (metonymy) where the cause (putting on the sash) stands for the effect (adornment), or as an isti'ara taba'iyya (derivative metaphor) where the action of wearing a sash is likened to adornment, and the verb is derived from the likeness. He then describes the physical sash in detail. Next, the commentator analyzes the phrase 'sudur al-kutub wa al-dafatir' (the chests of books and ledgers). He notes that 'sudur' is the plural of 'sadr' (chest), the place of the heart. When applied to books and ledgers, it is a isti'ara bil-kinnaya (metaphorical metonymy), likening the books to beautiful women who have chests adorned with sashes. Thus 'sudur' serves as takhyeel (imaginative element) and 'yutawashshahu' as tarshih (confirmatory element). He then presents an alternative interpretation: that 'sudur' here means the beginnings (awa'il) of the books, in which case the likening is between the openings of books and women, with 'yutawashshahu' being the imaginative element. The commentator also defines 'kutub' and 'dafatir', noting that 'daftar' is a ledger for accounts, and its etymology is unknown. He then proceeds to parse the phrase 'hamd Allah' (praise of God) as the predicate of 'inna', and 'jalla jalaluhu' as a report of magnification. Finally, he remarks on the word 'ala' (blessings) and notes that the choice of 'dafatir' (ledgers) over 'rasa'il' (letters) is because ledgers are often base, so using them in the context of praise is a deliberate choice for rhetorical effect. The entire commentary serves to unpack the layered metaphorical and rhetorical features of the opening line.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Metaphorical Interpretation of Opening Praises
Commentators explain the metaphor in the opening praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the types of metaphors (isti'ara makniyya, tashbih) used in the introduction of the Tadhheeb sharh and explain how the commentators unpack them.

Core Idea
The opening praise is a layered metaphor: books as women, praise as a sash.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi and al-Attar meticulously analyze the author's first phrase. They show that words like 'sudur' (chests/foreparts) and 'yatwashshahu' (adorns) are not just decorative but part of a sophisticated metaphorical structure. This lesson will walk you through their method, a key skill for reading Islamic scholarly texts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the likening (tashbih) or metaphor
Look for key terms: صُدُور, يَتَوَشَّحُ, وشاح.
The literal definitions of these terms are provided by the commentator.
2
Classify the metaphor type
Determine if it is isti'ara makniyya (implicit) or tashbih baligh (eloquent simile).
The commentator uses 'isti'ara bil-kinaya' and 'tariq al-makniyya'.
3
Identify the 'imaginative' (takhayyul) and 'supplementary' (tarshih) elements
Note which part is fanciful and which extends the metaphor.
'Sudur' is imaginative; 'yatwashshahu' is supplementary.
4
Consider alternative interpretations
When 'sudur' means beginnings vs. chests, the metaphor changes.
Both interpretations are explored.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الشارح

حَمْدُ اللهِ - جل جلاله - على آلائِهِ

الدسوقي

إلباس الوشاح، وهو شيءٌ يُتَّخذُ مِن أديم؛ أي: جِلْدِ عريض، ويُرصع بالجواهر، تجعله المرأة بين عاتقها وخاصرتها ؛ بأن تلبسه كلبس السَّيف، والخاصرة: ما لانَ من الجانب، والعاتق : المنكبُ (قَوْلُهُ: صُدُوْرُ جمعُ صدر ؛ محلُّ القلب، فيكون في الكتب والدفاتر، استعارة بالكناية؛ حيثُ شبههما بالنِّساءِ الحِسَانِ بجامع الحُسْنِ والشَّرفِ، وصدورُ تَخييل، ويتوشّح ترشيح ، وشبه حمد الله بالوشاح على طريق المكنية، والتوشيح تخييل، ويحتمل أن يُراد بصدورِ الكُتُبِ : أوائِلُها، فيكون شبه أوائل الكتب بالنِّساء على طريق المكنية، ويتوشَّحُ تخييل (قَوْلُهُ : الْكُتُبِ جمعُ كتاب، وهو الصَّحيفةُ والدَّفاتر جمع دفتر، وهو جريدة ، الحساب ؛ أي: الورقُ الَّذي يكتب فيه الحساب بينَ النَّاس، ولم يعرف اشتقاقُ الدَّفترِ مِن أيّ شيء (قَوْلُهُ : حَمْدُ اللهِ) خبر «إِنَّ» ؛ أي : ثناؤه . (قَوْلُهُ : جَلَّ جَلالُهُ من باب الإخبارِ ؛ أي: عَظُمَتْ عَظَمتُهُ؛ أَيْ: تَنَزَّهَتْ عَظَمَتُهُ عَنِ النَّقائص، أو إنَّه إنشاء لإظهار ذلك.

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى آلَائِهِ مُتعلّق بحمد، وَمَا بينَهما ،مُعترض، قصد به التنزيه، وهو جمع إلى بالقصر، وهو النعمة، فالهمزة الأولى همزةُ الجمع، والثانية فاء المفردِ

العطار

الرَّسائل موافقةُ السَّجع، وحملها على المعنى الحقيقي كما قالوا بعيد في هذا المقام؛ إذ الدَّفاتر ليست من الأمور ذواتِ البالِ الَّتي تُصدَّرُ بالحمدِ ، بل كثيراً ما يُذكَرُ فيها ما ينزّه الحمد عن أن يصدرَ بهِ فيها ؛ كدفاتر المظالم والمعاملاتِ، ويَتَوَشَّحُ : مجاز مُرسَلٌ تبعي أو استعارةٌ مُصرَّحةٌ تبعيَّةٌ ليَتزيَّن، علاقتَهُ السَّببيَّةُ أو المشابهة أو استعارة تخييلية للمكنية في قوله : (بذكره) بتشبيه الذكرِ بالوشاح، ثمَّ إِنْ أُريدَ مِن صُدورِ الكتب أوائلُها ؛ فالكلام على حقيقته، وإنْ أُريد بها محلُّ القَلبِ؛ فالإضافةُ مِن قبيل إضافة المشبه به للمشبه بجامع الاشتمال على كلِّ نفيس، أو الكتب استعارة تخييلية، لتشبيهها بإنسان له صدرُ ، والصدور تخييل، ويتوشَّحُ ترشيح .

قُلِبَت أَلِفاً ؛ دَفْعاً للنّقل باجتماع همزتَيْنِ .

(قَوْلُهُ : الْمُزْهِرَةِ الرِّياضِ ) جمعُ ،روضة، وهي البستان؛ أي: آلائهِ الَّتِي كَالرِّياض المزهرة بجامعِ الحُسْنِ في كلِّ ؛ لأنّ كُلًّا مِنَ النّعم بمعنى المنعم به، والرياض ،حسن، وَقَوْلُهُ : المزهرة؛ أي: التي بدأ بها زهرها، ويحتمل أن آلاءهُ تعالى شُبِّهَتْ بمدن ذاتِ رياض على طريق المكنيَّةِ، والرّياضُ تَخييل لا يُقال : إِنَّ هذه الجملة لا تفيدُ الابتداء بالحمدلة، بل لا تفيد الإتيان بها، فضلاً عن كونه مبتدأ بِهِ؛ لأنَّها إنَّما تفيدُ الإخبار بأنَّ حَمْدَ اللهِ أحسنُ الكلام الذي ينطقُ بِهِ اللّسانُ؛ لأنَّا نقول : الإخبار بذلك ثناه على الله باللازم؛ لأنَّه إذا أثنى على حمدِ اللهِ؛ فقد حمد الله لزوماً، فيكونُ حمداً واقعاً في الابتداء، على أنَّ الرَّاجِحَ أنَّ الإخبار بالحَمدِ حَمْدٌ، فتأمل. (قَوْلُهُ: وَشُكْرُهُ) عطفٌ على حَمْد .

(قَوْلُهُ: عَمَّ نَوَالُهُ) أي : عطاؤُه جميع المخلوقات، وهذه جمله معترضةٌ قصد بها الدُّعاء .

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى نَعْمَائِهِ) متعلّق بشكره، وهو جمع نعمة، ولم يقل : على آلائِهِ؛ تفتُناً، تحمد عاقبته ؛ أي: تكون عاقبته حميدةً؛ أي: دخول الجنَّة .

العطار

والنّعمةُ : كُلّ ملائم (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى آلَائِهِ المُزْهِرَةِ الرِّياضِ متعلّق بحمدٍ على أنَّه ظرفُ لغو، والآلاء: النّعَمُ، جمعُ إِلَى بالقصر وفتح الهمزة والكسر ، وفي كلام بعضهم : إِنَّ النِّعمة: النّعَمُ الباطنة وملائماتُها، والآلاءُ : النّعَمُ الظَّاهرة كالحواس الخمس وملائماتها، والأصلُ : أألاء بوزنِ أفعال ؛ أَبدِلَت الهمزةُ الثَّانيةُ الَّتي هي فاء الكلمةِ أَلِفاً لثقل الهمزتين، والرّياضُ : البساتين، أصله رواض، قُلبت الواو ياء؛ لوقوعها إثر كسرة، والكلام تشبيه بليغ ؛ أي: الآلاء التي هي كالرِّياض المزهرة، أو استعارة مكنيَّةٌ بأنْ تُشبَّةَ الآلاءُ بأرض حسنةٍ ذاتِ بساتين مُزهرة، والرِّياضُ تخييل.

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى نَعْمَائِهِ) فيها لغتَانِ؛ فتحُ النُّونِ ،وضمها، فَإِنْ فُتِحَتِ النُّونُ؛ مدَدْتَه للنعمة . كما هنا، وإِنْ ضُمَّتْ ؛ قصرْتَه، وهي إما بمعنى الإنعام، أو اسم جمع
Introduction of the commentator

Praise of God - may His majesty be exalted - for His blessings

Al-Dasuqi: Wearing the sash, and it is something taken from leather; that is, wide skin, and it is studded with jewels, the woman places it between her shoulder and her hip; by wearing it like wearing a sword, and the hip is what is soft of the side, and the shoulder is the collarbone. His saying: 'Sudur' is the plural of 'sadr' (chest), the place of the heart, so it is in books and notebooks, a metaphor by metonymy; where he likened them to beautiful women in the commonality of beauty and honor, and 'sudur' is imaginative, and 'yatwashshahu' is supplementary. And he likened praise of God to the sash in the way of implicit metaphor, and 'al-tawshih' is imaginative. And it is possible that by 'sudur al-kutub' is meant their beginnings, so it would be likening the beginnings of books to women in the way of implicit metaphor, and 'yatwashshahu' is imaginative. His saying: 'al-kutub' is the plural of 'kitab', and it is the sheet, and 'al-dafatir' is the plural of 'daftar', and it is the ledger of accounts; that is, the paper on which accounts are written among people, and the derivation of 'daftar' is not known from anything. His saying: 'Hamd Allah' is the predicate of 'inna'; that is, praising Him. His saying: 'Jalla jalaluhu' is of the category of informing; that is, His greatness is great; meaning: His greatness is exalted above deficiencies, or it is a construction to manifest that. His saying: 'Ala alaihi' is attached to 'hamd', and what is between them is parenthetical, intended for exaltation, and it is the plural of 'ila' with shortening, and it is blessing, so the first hamza is the plural hamza, and the second is the fa of the singular.

Al-Attar: The letters are consonant with the rhyme, and taking them in the real meaning, as they said, is far-fetched in this context; for notebooks are not among matters of importance that are prefaced with praise, but rather often what is mentioned in them exalts praise from being prefaced with it; like notebooks of injustices and transactions. And 'yatwashshahu' is a transferred metonymy or an explicit derivative metaphor for adornment, its relation is causality or similarity, or an imaginative metaphor for the implicit metaphor in his saying: 'by His mention' likening mention to the sash. Then if by 'sudur al-kutub' their beginnings are meant, the speech is in its real sense; and if the place of the heart is meant, then the addition is of the type of adding the thing likened to the thing likened to it in the commonality of containing every precious thing, or the books are an imaginative metaphor, likening them to a human who has a chest, and 'al-sudur' is imaginative, and 'yatwashshahu' is supplementary.

It was turned into an alif; to prevent the shift due to the meeting of two hamzas.

His saying: 'al-muzhirati al-riyad' is the plural of 'rawda', and it is the garden; that is, His blessings which are like flourishing gardens in the commonality of beauty in everything; because each of the blessings is in the sense of the thing blessed with, and gardens are beautiful. And his saying: 'al-muzhira'; that is, whose flower has begun to bloom. And it is possible that His blessings - exalted is He - are likened to cities with gardens in the way of implicit metaphor, and 'al-riyad' is imaginative. It cannot be said: this sentence does not indicate the initiation of praise, rather it does not indicate coming with it, let alone its being initiated with it; because it only indicates the information that the praise of God is the best speech that the tongue utters; for we say: in forming that information is praising God by necessity; because if he praises the praise of God, then he has praised God necessarily, so it is praise occurring at the beginning, and the preponderant view is that informing of praise is praise. So contemplate. His saying: 'wa shukruhu' is coordinated with 'hamd'. His saying: 'Amma nawaluhu' means His giving encompasses all creatures, and this is an parenthetical sentence intended for supplication. His saying: 'ala na'maihi' is attached to 'shukr', and it is the plural of 'ni'ma', and he did not say: 'ala alaihi' for variety, its end is praiseworthy; that is, its end is good; that is, entering Paradise.

Al-Attar: And al-ni'ma is everything agreeable. His saying: 'ala alaihi al-muzhirati al-riyad' is attached to 'hamd' as a non-restrictive prepositional phrase, and al-ala': are blessings, the plural of 'ila' with shortening and opening of the hamza and kasra, and in the speech of some: al-ni'ma is the inner blessings and their agreeable things, and al-ala' is the outer blessings like the five senses and their agreeable things. And the original is 'a'ala' with the measure of 'af'al'; the second hamza which is the fa of the word was changed to an alif due to the weight of two hamzas. And al-riyad: gardens, originally 'rawad', the waw was turned into a ya' due to its occurrence after a kasra. And the speech is an eloquent similitude; that is, the blessings which are like flourishing gardens, or an implicit metaphor by which the blessings are likened to a good land with flourishing gardens, and al-riyad is imaginative.

His saying: 'ala na'maihi' has two pronunciations: opening the nun or damma it. If the nun is opened, you lengthen it for the blessing, as here. If it is damma, you shorten it, and it is either in the sense of bestowing blessings or a collective noun.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is from the introduction of the commentary (sharh) on the text 'Tadhheeb'. The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-Attar, are explaining the author's opening praise of God. They begin by unpacking the metaphorical language used in the phrase 'sudur al-kutub wa al-dafatir' (the chests/foreparts of books and notebooks). Al-Dasuqi explains that 'sudur' (plural of sadr) can mean the chest (the place of the heart) or the beginning of something. The author likens books and notebooks to beautiful women, with 'sudur' as the imaginative part and 'yatwashshahu' (he adorns) as the supplementary. The praise of God is likened to a sash (wishah) worn by women, an implicit metaphor. Alternatively, if 'sudur' means the beginnings, then the books are implicitly likened to women. Al-Dasuqi then defines 'kutub' and 'dafatir', noting that the etymology of 'daftar' is unknown. He then clarifies that 'hamd Allah' is the predicate of an implied 'inna', and 'jalla jalaluhu' is an expression of exaltation. 'Ala alaihi' is attached to 'hamd', with 'alaihi' being the plural of 'ila' (blessing). The parenthetical phrase serves to exalt God.

Al-Attar then adds that taking the phrase literally is far-fetched because notebooks often contain mundane things, not worthy of praise. He analyzes 'yatwashshahu' as a derived metaphor or metonymy. He discusses two interpretations: if 'sudur' means beginnings, the wording is literal; if it means chests, it's a metaphor comparing books to humans. The addition is one of likening. He also notes the transformation of hamzas into alif for ease of pronunciation.

Continuing, al-Dasuqi explains 'al-muzhirati al-riyad' (the flourishing gardens) as describing the blessings (alaihi). The blessings are likened to flourishing gardens in beauty. Some interpret this as an implicit metaphor comparing blessings to cities with gardens. He then anticipates an objection: the sentence does not seem to initiate praise, but he argues that informing that 'praise of God is the best speech' is itself praise because praising God's praise is a form of praising God necessarily. The preponderant view is that such information counts as praise. Then he explains 'wa shukruhu' (and His thanks) and 'amma nawaluhu' (His giving encompasses), the latter being a parenthetical supplication. The phrase 'ala na'maihi' is attached to 'shukr', and the choice of 'na'maihi' instead of 'alaihi' is for stylistic variety, leading to a praiseworthy end (Paradise).

Al-Attar further distinguishes between 'ni'ma' (inner blessings) and 'ala' (outer blessings), and provides the morphological analysis of 'ala' as originating from 'a'ala' (with two hamzas) where the second hamza is turned into alif. He also explains 'riyad' as gardens, with a vowel shift. The overall description is a beautiful simile or implicit metaphor. Finally, he notes the two possible pronunciations of 'na'maihi' (with fatha or damma on the nun) and their meanings: if fatha, it is the plural of 'ni'ma' (blessings); if damma, it is either a verbal noun (bestowing blessings) or a collective noun. The passage thus methodically unpacks the author's introductory phrasing, demonstrating the interplay of literal and metaphorical meanings, and defending the structure of the praise.

وشاح
حمد

A) Books are likened to swords

Key Takeaway
The commentators' metaphorical analysis reveals that every word in the opening praise is purposeful and rhetorically crafted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Logical Force of Praise: Informing as Commending
Commentator defends the opening sentence as genuine praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that a statement informing that something is praiseworthy constitutes an act of praise, and apply this logical inference to similar cases in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Praising the praise of God is a necessary form of praising God.

Al-Dasuqi anticipates an objection: how can a declarative sentence like 'Praise of God is the best speech' be an act of praise? He argues that informing about the praiseworthiness of praise is itself praise, because it involves praising the praise, which logically implies praising God. This lesson dissects that inference and its implications for the use of such phrases in scholarly works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The sentence appears to be a statement of fact, not an act of praise. It does not directly praise God but describes praise.
The objector says the sentence does not initiate praise.
2
Extract al-Dasuqi's response
He claims that informing that praise of God is beautiful is praising God by necessity (لزوماً).
Because praising the praise of God is a type of praising God.
3
Analyze the logical necessity
If one praises the praise of God (i.e., extols it as good), then one has praised God, since the praise is directed towards God. This is a necessary implication.
This is akin to saying: if you say 'This gift is wonderful', you are praising the giver indirectly.
4
Conclude the validity
Thus, the opening sentence is a valid act of praise, and it initiates the work with praise.
Al-Dasuqi also notes the preponderant view that informing of praise is praise.

Source Text

مُقَدِّمَةُ الشارح

حَمْدُ اللهِ - جل جلاله - على آلائِهِ

الدسوقي

إلباس الوشاح، وهو شيءٌ يُتَّخذُ مِن أديم؛ أي: جِلْدِ عريض، ويُرصع بالجواهر، تجعله المرأة بين عاتقها وخاصرتها ؛ بأن تلبسه كلبس السَّيف، والخاصرة: ما لانَ من الجانب، والعاتق : المنكبُ (قَوْلُهُ: صُدُوْرُ جمعُ صدر ؛ محلُّ القلب، فيكون في الكتب والدفاتر، استعارة بالكناية؛ حيثُ شبههما بالنِّساءِ الحِسَانِ بجامع الحُسْنِ والشَّرفِ، وصدورُ تَخييل، ويتوشّح ترشيح ، وشبه حمد الله بالوشاح على طريق المكنية، والتوشيح تخييل، ويحتمل أن يُراد بصدورِ الكُتُبِ : أوائِلُها، فيكون شبه أوائل الكتب بالنِّساء على طريق المكنية، ويتوشَّحُ تخييل (قَوْلُهُ : الْكُتُبِ جمعُ كتاب، وهو الصَّحيفةُ والدَّفاتر جمع دفتر، وهو جريدة ، الحساب ؛ أي: الورقُ الَّذي يكتب فيه الحساب بينَ النَّاس، ولم يعرف اشتقاقُ الدَّفترِ مِن أيّ شيء (قَوْلُهُ : حَمْدُ اللهِ) خبر «إِنَّ» ؛ أي : ثناؤه . (قَوْلُهُ : جَلَّ جَلالُهُ من باب الإخبارِ ؛ أي: عَظُمَتْ عَظَمتُهُ؛ أَيْ: تَنَزَّهَتْ عَظَمَتُهُ عَنِ النَّقائص، أو إنَّه إنشاء لإظهار ذلك.

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى آلَائِهِ مُتعلّق بحمد، وَمَا بينَهما ،مُعترض، قصد به التنزيه، وهو جمع إلى بالقصر، وهو النعمة، فالهمزة الأولى همزةُ الجمع، والثانية فاء المفردِ

العطار

الرَّسائل موافقةُ السَّجع، وحملها على المعنى الحقيقي كما قالوا بعيد في هذا المقام؛ إذ الدَّفاتر ليست من الأمور ذواتِ البالِ الَّتي تُصدَّرُ بالحمدِ ، بل كثيراً ما يُذكَرُ فيها ما ينزّه الحمد عن أن يصدرَ بهِ فيها ؛ كدفاتر المظالم والمعاملاتِ، ويَتَوَشَّحُ : مجاز مُرسَلٌ تبعي أو استعارةٌ مُصرَّحةٌ تبعيَّةٌ ليَتزيَّن، علاقتَهُ السَّببيَّةُ أو المشابهة أو استعارة تخييلية للمكنية في قوله : (بذكره) بتشبيه الذكرِ بالوشاح، ثمَّ إِنْ أُريدَ مِن صُدورِ الكتب أوائلُها ؛ فالكلام على حقيقته، وإنْ أُريد بها محلُّ القَلبِ؛ فالإضافةُ مِن قبيل إضافة المشبه به للمشبه بجامع الاشتمال على كلِّ نفيس، أو الكتب استعارة تخييلية، لتشبيهها بإنسان له صدرُ ، والصدور تخييل، ويتوشَّحُ ترشيح .

قُلِبَت أَلِفاً ؛ دَفْعاً للنّقل باجتماع همزتَيْنِ .

(قَوْلُهُ : الْمُزْهِرَةِ الرِّياضِ ) جمعُ ،روضة، وهي البستان؛ أي: آلائهِ الَّتِي كَالرِّياض المزهرة بجامعِ الحُسْنِ في كلِّ ؛ لأنّ كُلًّا مِنَ النّعم بمعنى المنعم به، والرياض ،حسن، وَقَوْلُهُ : المزهرة؛ أي: التي بدأ بها زهرها، ويحتمل أن آلاءهُ تعالى شُبِّهَتْ بمدن ذاتِ رياض على طريق المكنيَّةِ، والرّياضُ تَخييل لا يُقال : إِنَّ هذه الجملة لا تفيدُ الابتداء بالحمدلة، بل لا تفيد الإتيان بها، فضلاً عن كونه مبتدأ بِهِ؛ لأنَّها إنَّما تفيدُ الإخبار بأنَّ حَمْدَ اللهِ أحسنُ الكلام الذي ينطقُ بِهِ اللّسانُ؛ لأنَّا نقول : الإخبار بذلك ثناه على الله باللازم؛ لأنَّه إذا أثنى على حمدِ اللهِ؛ فقد حمد الله لزوماً، فيكونُ حمداً واقعاً في الابتداء، على أنَّ الرَّاجِحَ أنَّ الإخبار بالحَمدِ حَمْدٌ، فتأمل. (قَوْلُهُ: وَشُكْرُهُ) عطفٌ على حَمْد .

(قَوْلُهُ: عَمَّ نَوَالُهُ) أي : عطاؤُه جميع المخلوقات، وهذه جمله معترضةٌ قصد بها الدُّعاء .

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى نَعْمَائِهِ) متعلّق بشكره، وهو جمع نعمة، ولم يقل : على آلائِهِ؛ تفتُناً، تحمد عاقبته ؛ أي: تكون عاقبته حميدةً؛ أي: دخول الجنَّة .

العطار

والنّعمةُ : كُلّ ملائم (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى آلَائِهِ المُزْهِرَةِ الرِّياضِ متعلّق بحمدٍ على أنَّه ظرفُ لغو، والآلاء: النّعَمُ، جمعُ إِلَى بالقصر وفتح الهمزة والكسر ، وفي كلام بعضهم : إِنَّ النِّعمة: النّعَمُ الباطنة وملائماتُها، والآلاءُ : النّعَمُ الظَّاهرة كالحواس الخمس وملائماتها، والأصلُ : أألاء بوزنِ أفعال ؛ أَبدِلَت الهمزةُ الثَّانيةُ الَّتي هي فاء الكلمةِ أَلِفاً لثقل الهمزتين، والرّياضُ : البساتين، أصله رواض، قُلبت الواو ياء؛ لوقوعها إثر كسرة، والكلام تشبيه بليغ ؛ أي: الآلاء التي هي كالرِّياض المزهرة، أو استعارة مكنيَّةٌ بأنْ تُشبَّةَ الآلاءُ بأرض حسنةٍ ذاتِ بساتين مُزهرة، والرِّياضُ تخييل.

(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى نَعْمَائِهِ) فيها لغتَانِ؛ فتحُ النُّونِ ،وضمها، فَإِنْ فُتِحَتِ النُّونُ؛ مدَدْتَه للنعمة . كما هنا، وإِنْ ضُمَّتْ ؛ قصرْتَه، وهي إما بمعنى الإنعام، أو اسم جمع
Introduction of the commentator

Praise of God - may His majesty be exalted - for His blessings

Al-Dasuqi: Wearing the sash, and it is something taken from leather; that is, wide skin, and it is studded with jewels, the woman places it between her shoulder and her hip; by wearing it like wearing a sword, and the hip is what is soft of the side, and the shoulder is the collarbone. His saying: 'Sudur' is the plural of 'sadr' (chest), the place of the heart, so it is in books and notebooks, a metaphor by metonymy; where he likened them to beautiful women in the commonality of beauty and honor, and 'sudur' is imaginative, and 'yatwashshahu' is supplementary. And he likened praise of God to the sash in the way of implicit metaphor, and 'al-tawshih' is imaginative. And it is possible that by 'sudur al-kutub' is meant their beginnings, so it would be likening the beginnings of books to women in the way of implicit metaphor, and 'yatwashshahu' is imaginative. His saying: 'al-kutub' is the plural of 'kitab', and it is the sheet, and 'al-dafatir' is the plural of 'daftar', and it is the ledger of accounts; that is, the paper on which accounts are written among people, and the derivation of 'daftar' is not known from anything. His saying: 'Hamd Allah' is the predicate of 'inna'; that is, praising Him. His saying: 'Jalla jalaluhu' is of the category of informing; that is, His greatness is great; meaning: His greatness is exalted above deficiencies, or it is a construction to manifest that. His saying: 'Ala alaihi' is attached to 'hamd', and what is between them is parenthetical, intended for exaltation, and it is the plural of 'ila' with shortening, and it is blessing, so the first hamza is the plural hamza, and the second is the fa of the singular.

Al-Attar: The letters are consonant with the rhyme, and taking them in the real meaning, as they said, is far-fetched in this context; for notebooks are not among matters of importance that are prefaced with praise, but rather often what is mentioned in them exalts praise from being prefaced with it; like notebooks of injustices and transactions. And 'yatwashshahu' is a transferred metonymy or an explicit derivative metaphor for adornment, its relation is causality or similarity, or an imaginative metaphor for the implicit metaphor in his saying: 'by His mention' likening mention to the sash. Then if by 'sudur al-kutub' their beginnings are meant, the speech is in its real sense; and if the place of the heart is meant, then the addition is of the type of adding the thing likened to the thing likened to it in the commonality of containing every precious thing, or the books are an imaginative metaphor, likening them to a human who has a chest, and 'al-sudur' is imaginative, and 'yatwashshahu' is supplementary.

It was turned into an alif; to prevent the shift due to the meeting of two hamzas.

His saying: 'al-muzhirati al-riyad' is the plural of 'rawda', and it is the garden; that is, His blessings which are like flourishing gardens in the commonality of beauty in everything; because each of the blessings is in the sense of the thing blessed with, and gardens are beautiful. And his saying: 'al-muzhira'; that is, whose flower has begun to bloom. And it is possible that His blessings - exalted is He - are likened to cities with gardens in the way of implicit metaphor, and 'al-riyad' is imaginative. It cannot be said: this sentence does not indicate the initiation of praise, rather it does not indicate coming with it, let alone its being initiated with it; because it only indicates the information that the praise of God is the best speech that the tongue utters; for we say: in forming that information is praising God by necessity; because if he praises the praise of God, then he has praised God necessarily, so it is praise occurring at the beginning, and the preponderant view is that informing of praise is praise. So contemplate. His saying: 'wa shukruhu' is coordinated with 'hamd'. His saying: 'Amma nawaluhu' means His giving encompasses all creatures, and this is an parenthetical sentence intended for supplication. His saying: 'ala na'maihi' is attached to 'shukr', and it is the plural of 'ni'ma', and he did not say: 'ala alaihi' for variety, its end is praiseworthy; that is, its end is good; that is, entering Paradise.

Al-Attar: And al-ni'ma is everything agreeable. His saying: 'ala alaihi al-muzhirati al-riyad' is attached to 'hamd' as a non-restrictive prepositional phrase, and al-ala': are blessings, the plural of 'ila' with shortening and opening of the hamza and kasra, and in the speech of some: al-ni'ma is the inner blessings and their agreeable things, and al-ala' is the outer blessings like the five senses and their agreeable things. And the original is 'a'ala' with the measure of 'af'al'; the second hamza which is the fa of the word was changed to an alif due to the weight of two hamzas. And al-riyad: gardens, originally 'rawad', the waw was turned into a ya' due to its occurrence after a kasra. And the speech is an eloquent similitude; that is, the blessings which are like flourishing gardens, or an implicit metaphor by which the blessings are likened to a good land with flourishing gardens, and al-riyad is imaginative.

His saying: 'ala na'maihi' has two pronunciations: opening the nun or damma it. If the nun is opened, you lengthen it for the blessing, as here. If it is damma, you shorten it, and it is either in the sense of bestowing blessings or a collective noun.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is from the introduction of the commentary (sharh) on the text 'Tadhheeb'. The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-Attar, are explaining the author's opening praise of God. They begin by unpacking the metaphorical language used in the phrase 'sudur al-kutub wa al-dafatir' (the chests/foreparts of books and notebooks). Al-Dasuqi explains that 'sudur' (plural of sadr) can mean the chest (the place of the heart) or the beginning of something. The author likens books and notebooks to beautiful women, with 'sudur' as the imaginative part and 'yatwashshahu' (he adorns) as the supplementary. The praise of God is likened to a sash (wishah) worn by women, an implicit metaphor. Alternatively, if 'sudur' means the beginnings, then the books are implicitly likened to women. Al-Dasuqi then defines 'kutub' and 'dafatir', noting that the etymology of 'daftar' is unknown. He then clarifies that 'hamd Allah' is the predicate of an implied 'inna', and 'jalla jalaluhu' is an expression of exaltation. 'Ala alaihi' is attached to 'hamd', with 'alaihi' being the plural of 'ila' (blessing). The parenthetical phrase serves to exalt God.

Al-Attar then adds that taking the phrase literally is far-fetched because notebooks often contain mundane things, not worthy of praise. He analyzes 'yatwashshahu' as a derived metaphor or metonymy. He discusses two interpretations: if 'sudur' means beginnings, the wording is literal; if it means chests, it's a metaphor comparing books to humans. The addition is one of likening. He also notes the transformation of hamzas into alif for ease of pronunciation.

Continuing, al-Dasuqi explains 'al-muzhirati al-riyad' (the flourishing gardens) as describing the blessings (alaihi). The blessings are likened to flourishing gardens in beauty. Some interpret this as an implicit metaphor comparing blessings to cities with gardens. He then anticipates an objection: the sentence does not seem to initiate praise, but he argues that informing that 'praise of God is the best speech' is itself praise because praising God's praise is a form of praising God necessarily. The preponderant view is that such information counts as praise. Then he explains 'wa shukruhu' (and His thanks) and 'amma nawaluhu' (His giving encompasses), the latter being a parenthetical supplication. The phrase 'ala na'maihi' is attached to 'shukr', and the choice of 'na'maihi' instead of 'alaihi' is for stylistic variety, leading to a praiseworthy end (Paradise).

Al-Attar further distinguishes between 'ni'ma' (inner blessings) and 'ala' (outer blessings), and provides the morphological analysis of 'ala' as originating from 'a'ala' (with two hamzas) where the second hamza is turned into alif. He also explains 'riyad' as gardens, with a vowel shift. The overall description is a beautiful simile or implicit metaphor. Finally, he notes the two possible pronunciations of 'na'maihi' (with fatha or damma on the nun) and their meanings: if fatha, it is the plural of 'ni'ma' (blessings); if damma, it is either a verbal noun (bestowing blessings) or a collective noun. The passage thus methodically unpacks the author's introductory phrasing, demonstrating the interplay of literal and metaphorical meanings, and defending the structure of the praise.

Checkpoint
What is the logical relationship al-Dasuqi uses to defend the opening sentence as praise?

Key Takeaway
In Islamic scholarship, a statement that describes praise as good can itself be an act of praise through necessary implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a similar argument in the opening of another Islamic text (e.g., 'الحمد لله الذي بنعمته تتم الصالحات') and explain how it functions as praise using the same reasoning.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
Distinguishing Ālā’ from Na‘mā’: Outer and Inner Blessings
Commentary on rhetorical features of praise terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ālā’ (outer blessings) and na‘mā’ (inner favors) based on the commentary, and identify the rhetorical devices used to describe them.

Core Idea
God's outer blessings are gardens; inner favors are cisterns — a vivid rhetorical contrast.

In the opening invocation, the author praises God for His blessings (ālā’) and favors (na‘mā’). The commentators unpack these terms, revealing a subtle distinction: ālā’ are outer, manifest blessings like the senses, while na‘mā’ are inner, spiritual favors. They use powerful similes and metaphors to make the distinction memorable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Arabic phrases
Locate 'عَلَى آلَائِهِ المُزْهِرَةِ الرِّياضِ' (for His flourishing gardens of blessings) and 'عَلَى نَعْمَائِهِ المُتْرَعَةِ الحِيَاضِ' (for His filled cisterns of favors). These are the core images.
Both phrases are attached to the verbs 'ḥamd' (praise) and 'shukr' (thanks) respectively.
2
Understand the literal meanings
'Ālā’' means outer blessings, and 'riyāḍ' means gardens; 'na‘mā’' means inner favors, and 'ḥiyāḍ' means cisterns. The commentators explain the metaphors: flourishing gardens filled with blossom, and cisterns filled with water.
3
Recognize the rhetorical forms
The descriptions are either tashbīh balīgh (compelling simile) where blessings are directly compared to gardens/cisterns, or isti‘āra makaniyya (implicit metaphor) where the blessings are likened to lands with gardens/cisterns, and the gardens/cisterns are imaginative (takhayyīl) elements.
This level of analysis is typical in balāgha (rhetoric) and shows how logic texts incorporate literary precision.
4
Distinguish the definitions
The commentators cite a view: 'ni‘ma' (favor) refers to inner blessings and agreeable things, while 'ālā’' refers to outer blessings like the five senses and their agreeable things. This distinction is key to understanding the theological nuance.
Not all scholars agree; the text presents it as one interpretation.
5
Note the morphological derivation
'Ālā’' is derived from 'a’ālā’' (pattern af‘āl) with the second hamza changed to alif due to the heaviness of two hamzas. 'Riyāḍ' originally 'rawāḍ' with waw changed to yā due to kasra. These details show the commentators' attention to linguistic roots.

Source Text
